Nikah — Marriage

10 min
Mahr for Non-Muslim Marriages: Harbi vs Dhimmi Distinction
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mahr for non-Muslim marriages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mahr for Christian marriages valid in their religion and distinguish between harbi and dhimmi couples, explaining the proofs of Abu Hanifa, the Sahibayn, and Imam Zufar.

Core Idea
Islamic authority determines mahr obligation: absent for harbis, present for dhimmis only in universal prohibitions.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a Christian marriage that is valid in their religion (e.g., with carrion as mahr or no mahr) entitles the wife to mahr under Islamic law. The ruling hinges on the status of the couple: are they harbi (from Dar al-Harb) or dhimmi (under Muslim rule)? Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn make a sharp distinction, while Imam Zufar argues for a general application of the Shari'ah requirement of mahr. Let's work through the positions and their proofs.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on harbi couples
Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn agree that for two warring non-Muslims (harbiyan) in Dar al-Harb, no mahr is due whether consummation, divorce, or death occurs. Identify the core rationale: lack of Islamic authority over them.
The Sahibayn's agreement is on the ruling, though their proof differs slightly from Abu Hanifa's.
2
Differentiate the dhimmi case
For a dhimmi woman married on the same invalid terms, Abu Hanifa rules: mahr al-mithl if consummated or husband dies, and mut'ah if divorced before consummation. The Sahibayn concur. Note the basis: dhimmis are under Muslim authority in transactional matters.
The mahr here is not the agreed invalid mahr but the equivalent of her status.
3
Present Zufar's opposing view
Imam Zufar holds that mahr al-mithl is due even for harbis. Identify his proof: the Shari'ah established marriage only with wealth (mahr), and this ruling is general, applying to all marriages.
This is a strict textual approach, ignoring the jurisdictional limitation.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to Zufar
The Hanafi response is twofold: (a) people of war are not bound by Islamic rulings and there is no authority to enforce; (b) dhimmis are bound only in matters they have undertaken (like riba) or universal prohibitions (zina), not in religious ceremonies like marriage. Thus, harbis are completely exempt; dhimmis are exempt in matters of religious belief.
The authority to enforce is severed by difference of abode (harbi) or by the dhimma contract itself (dhimmi).
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical consequence: a Muslim judge will not award any mahr to harbi couples, but will award mahr al-mithl or mut'ah to dhimmi couples depending on circumstances. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, as it accounts for jurisdiction.
This ruling applies only when the case comes before a Muslim court.

Section... And when a Christian man marries a Christian woman on [the condition of] carrion or without mahr, and that is permissible in their religion, and he consummated with her or divorced her before consummation or died away from her, then she has no mahr. Similarly, the two warring people [i.e., combatant non-Muslims] in Dar al-Harb. This is according to Abu Hanifa, and it is the saying of the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] regarding the two warring people. As for the dhimmi woman, she has her mahr al-mithl if he died or consummated, and mut'ah if he divorced before consummation. Zufar said: She has mahr al-mithl in the case of warring people as well, because the Shari'ah did not prescribe seeking marriage except with wealth, and this Shari'ah occurred generally, so the ruling is established generally. And according to the two [Sahibayn], the people of war are not bound by the rulings of Islam, and the authority to enforce is severed due to difference of abode, unlike the people of dhimma because they have taken on our rulings regarding transactions, like usury and fornication, and the authority to enforce is realized due to unity of abode. And according to Abu Hanifa: the people of dhimma do not take on our rulings in religious matters and in what they believe contrary to us in transactions, and the authority to enforce is by sword or by argument, and all that is severed from them by virtue of the dhimma contract, for we are commanded to leave them and what they believe, so they become like the people of war, unlike fornication because it is forbidden in all religions, and usury is exempted from their contracts due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'He who practices usury has no covenant between us and him.' And his saying in the book: 'or without mahr' can imply negation of mahr or silence. And it has been said regarding carrion and silence: there are two narrations, and the correct is that all are subject to difference [of opinion]. Translation: And when a Christian man married a Christian woman on carrion or without mahr, although this is permissible in their religion, and he consummated with the woman or divorced her before consummation or died leaving her, then the woman has no mahr. And this is the same ruling for two warring people in Dar al-Harb. And this is according to Abu Hanifa, and this is the saying of the two [Sahibayn] regarding the two warring people. However, in the case of a dhimmi woman, she has her mahr al-mithl if he died leaving her or consummated with her, and mut'ah if he divorced before consummation. Imam Zufar said: The woman has mahr al-mithl in the case of warring people as well. Imam Zufar's proof is that the Shari'ah did not prescribe seeking marriage except with wealth, and this Shari'ah occurred generally, so the ruling is established generally. And the proof of the two [Sahibayn] is that the people of war are not bound by the rulings of Islam, and the authority to enforce is severed due to difference of abode, unlike the people of dhimma because they have taken on our rulings regarding transactions, like usury and fornication, and the authority to enforce is realized due to unity of abode. And Abu Hanifa's proof is that the people of dhimma do not take on our rulings in religious matters and in those matters which they believe contrary to us in transactions, and the authority to enforce is by sword or by argument, and all that is severed from them by virtue of the dhimma contract, for we are commanded to leave them and what they believe, so they become like the people of war, unlike fornication because it is forbidden in all religions, and usury is exempted from their contracts due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'He who practices usury has no covenant between us and him.' And his saying in the book: 'or without mahr' can imply negation of mahr or silence. And it has been said regarding carrion and silence: there are two narrations, and the correct is that all are subject to difference [of opinion].
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the mahr obligation in a marriage of non-Muslims that is valid in their religion but invalid in Islam (due to carrion as mahr or no mahr). The passage is a ruling with proof, specifically an expansion of the Hidayah commentary. There are three positions: (1) Abu Hanifa's view: for a Christian man and woman (both harbi) in Dar al-Harb, no mahr is due, whether consummation, divorce before consummation, or death. For dhimmi couples, mahr al-mithl is due if consummated or husband dies, and mut'ah if divorced before consummation. (2) The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) agree with Abu Hanifa regarding harbis but differ on the reasoning? Actually the text says 'and this is the saying of the two regarding warring people' meaning they agree on harbis. But for dhimmis, they agree on mahr al-mithl. Their proof: People of war are not bound by Islamic rulings and there is no authority to enforce due to different abode. Dhimmis, however, have undertaken our rulings in transactions (like usury and fornication) and are under our authority due to unity of abode. (3) Zufar holds that mahr al-mithl is due even for harbis, because the Shari'ah established marriage only with wealth, and this is general. The Hanafi response (from Abu Hanifa) is that dhimmis are not bound in religious matters or in what they believe contrary to us, including transactions. The authority to enforce is by sword or argument, but it is severed by the dhimma contract, so they are like harbis. Exceptions are fornication (haram in all religions) and usury (due to hadith). The passage also notes that 'on carrion' and 'without mahr' have two narrations: one negating mahr, one implying silence. The correct view is that all are subject to difference.

Key Terms
نصراني (nasrani): A Christian; here, a non-Muslim person who may be under different categories (harbi or dhimmi).
مهر مثل (mahr al-mithl): The mahr equivalent to what women of her status receive, determined by factors like family, beauty, etc.
ذمية (dhimmiyah): A non-Muslim woman living under Muslim protection (dhimma) and subject to certain Islamic laws.
حربي (harbi): A non-Muslim from a territory at war with Muslims (Dar al-Harb), not under Islamic rule.
متعہ (mut'ah): A consolatory gift given to a wife divorced before consummation when no mahr is specified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling for a dhimmi woman married on carrion (murdar) if the husband dies after consummation?
A) She gets no mahr
B) She gets mahr al-mithl
C) She gets mut'ah only
D) The marriage is invalid, so no rights

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between harbi and dhimmi non-Muslims: no mahr for harbis; mahr al-mithl or mut'ah for dhimmis depending on consummation.

Mahr for Non-Muslims: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Dhimmi and Harbi Women
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mahr for dhimmi and harbi women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi positions on mahr for dhimmi and harbi women and identify the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
A dhimmi woman gets mahr al-mithl; a harbi woman gets nothing — the ruling depends on acceptance of Islamic law.

This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute about mahr when non-Muslims marry in a way they consider valid but Islamic law does not (e.g., on carrion or with no mahr). The ruling varies depending on whether the woman is a dhimmi (protected non-Muslim) or a harbi (from enemy territory), and on which Hanafi Imam's view is followed. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi school's nuanced approach to the legal capacity of non-Muslims in transactional matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and its legal context
The marriage is contracted on carrion or without specifying a mahr, and the couple's religion considers it valid. The husband either consummates, divorces before consummation, or dies. Determine whether the woman is dhimmi or harbi.
2
State the three Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa: no mahr for either. Sahibayn: no mahr for harbi, but mahr al-mithl (or mut'ah) for dhimmi. Zufar: mahr al-mithl for both.
The Sahibayn's view is the mufta bihi.
3
Analyze the proofs for each position
Abu Hanifa argues dhimmis do not accept Islamic rulings in religious/transactional matters. Sahibayn distinguish harbis (no enforcement due to separate dar) from dhimmis (bound by our transactions). Zufar argues the Shari'ah requirement of mahr is universal.
Note the hadith on usury as an exception to the dhimmi exemption.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and its thamarah
The relied-upon position is that of the Sahibayn: a dhimmi woman gets mahr al-mithl (consummation/death) or mut'ah (pre-consumption divorce); a harbi woman gets nothing.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
5
Apply the ruling to a concrete scenario
Example: A dhimmi couple marries on carrion and the husband dies. According to the Sahibayn, the widow is entitled to mahr al-mithl. If the couple were harbi in dar al-harb, she gets nothing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نصرانی نے نصرانیہ سے مردار پر یا بغیر مہر کے نکاح کیا ، دخول کیا یا دخول سے پہلے طلاق دیدی یا شوہر فوت ہو گیا تو عورت کیلئے مہر نہی۔ فصل ....... واذا تزوج النصراني نصرانية على ميتة او على غير مهر وذلك في دينهم جائز ودخل بها اوطلقها قبل الدخول بها اومات عنها فليس لها مهر و كذلك الحربيان فى دار الحرب وهذا عندابي حنيفة وهو قولهما في الحربيين واما في الذمية فلها مهر مثلها ان مات عنها أو دخل بها والمتعة ان طلقها قبل الدخول وقال زفر لهامهر المثل في الحربيين ايضاله ان الشرع ما شرع ابتغاء النكاح الابالمال وهذا الشرع وقع عاما فيثبت الحكم على العموم ولهما ان اهل الحرب غير ملتزمين احكام الاسلام وولاية الالزام منقطعة لتباين الدار بخلاف اهل الذمة لانهم التزموا احكامنا فيما يرجع الى المعاملات كالربو او الزناء وولاية الالزام متحققة لاتحاد الدار ولابي حنيفة ان اهل الذمة لا يلتزمون احكامنا فى الديانات وفيما يعتقدون خلافه في المعاملات وولاية الالزام بالسيف او بالمحاجة وكلّ ذلك منقطع عنهم باعتبار عقدالذمة فانا امرنا بان نتركهم و مايدينون فصاروا كاهل الحرب بخلاف الزناء لانه حرام فى الاديان كلها والربوا مستثنى عن عقود هم لقوله عليه السلام الامن اربي فليس بيننا وبينه عهد وقوله فى الكتاب او على غير مهر يحتمل نفى المهر ويحتمل السكوت وقدقيل في الميتة والسكوت روايتان والاصح ان الكل على الخلاف
A Christian married a Christian woman on carrion or without a dower, and that is permissible in their religion, and he had intercourse with her, or divorced her before intercourse, or he died, so she has no dower. And similarly the two harbis in dar al-harb. And this is according to Abu Hanifa, and it is their (the Sahibayn's) statement regarding the harbis. But as for the dhimmi woman, she has her mahr al-mithl if he dies or has intercourse with her, and mut'ah if he divorces her before intercourse. And Zufar said: She has mahr al-mithl in the case of the harbis as well, because the Shari'ah did not prescribe seeking marriage except with wealth, and this Shari'ah occurred generally, so the ruling is established generally. And for them (the Sahibayn): the people of war are not bound by the rulings of Islam, and the authority of enforcement is severed due to difference of dar, unlike the people of dhimma because they have undertaken our rulings regarding transactions like usury or fornication, and the authority of enforcement is realized due to unity of dar. And for Abu Hanifa: the people of dhimma do not undertake our rulings in religious matters and in what they believe contrary to it in transactions, and the authority of enforcement is by the sword or by argument, and all that is severed from them due to the dhimma contract, for we have been commanded to leave them and their religion, so they became like the people of war, unlike fornication because it is forbidden in all religions, and usury is exempted from their contracts due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever engages in usury, there is no covenant between us and him.' And his statement in the book 'or without a dower' carries the possibility of negating the dower and the possibility of silence. And it has been said that regarding carrion and silence there are two narrations, and the correct is that all are subject to the dispute.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the mahr (dower) for non-Muslim women when a marriage is contracted on carrion (murdar) or without any specified mahr, under the condition that such a marriage is considered valid in their own faith. The discussion distinguishes between dhimmi (non-Muslims living under Islamic protection) and harbi (non-Muslims from hostile territory) women. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that in all such cases—whether the husband consummates the marriage, divorces before consummation, or dies—the woman is entitled to no mahr, regardless of whether she is dhimmi or harbi. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) differentiate: for harbi women, they agree with Abu Hanifa that no mahr is due; for dhimmi women, they rule that she is entitled to mahr al-mithl (the proper dower) if the husband consummates or dies, and mut'ah (consolation gift) if he divorces before consummation. Imam Zufar goes further, holding that even harbi women are entitled to mahr al-mithl in all scenarios. The passage delves into the reasoning: Zufar argues that the Shari'ah's requirement of wealth for marriage (based on the Quranic verse 'seeking with your wealth' and the general nature of the Prophet's mission) is universal, applying to Muslims and non-Muslims alike. The Sahibayn counter that harbis are not bound by Islamic rulings due to the separation of territories (tabayun al-dar), unlike dhimmis who have agreed to abide by Islamic transactional laws. Abu Hanifa retorts that dhimmis do not accept Islamic rulings in religious matters or even in transactions where their beliefs differ, and the authority to enforce such rulings is cut off by the dhimma contract (as Muslims are commanded to leave them to their religion). The only exceptions are fornication (because it is universally forbidden) and usury (which is excluded per the hadith). The passage concludes by noting that the phrase 'or without a dower' in the original text can mean either explicit negation of dower or silence regarding it, and while there are two narrations, the correct view is that all cases remain subject to the aforementioned disagreement. The Urdu commentary reinforces these positions and clarifies the basis for each imam's view, emphasizing that the ruling for dhimmis follows the Sahibayn while for harbis it follows Abu Hanifa (and the Sahibayn) against Zufar. The practical effect is that a dhimmi woman can claim mahr al-mithl, whereas a harbi woman cannot, according to the majority of the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
ذمی (dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under Islamic rule who has a covenant of protection.
حربی (harbi): A non-Muslim from hostile territory (dar al-harb) with no covenant.
مھر مثل (mahr al-mithl): The proper dower assessed by looking at the dowers of women similar to the bride (e.g., her peers).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), what is the ruling for a dhimmi woman married on carrion if the husband consummates the marriage?
A) She gets no mahr at all.
B) She gets mahr al-mithl.
C) She gets only mut'ah.
D) She gets exactly what was specified in the contract.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi distinguishes: dhimmi women get mahr al-mithl or mut'ah, harbi women get nothing, based on their acceptance of Islamic transactional law.

Mahr of Wine or Pork After Conversion: The Three Hanafi Positions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mahr valuation after conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on the mahr when dhimmi spouses convert, and explain the legal reasoning (illa) behind each position.

Core Idea
Conversion does not void the mahr, but the three Hanafi imams differ on how to convert it into a permissible equivalent.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute. The case: a dhimmi couple marries with wine or pork as mahr, then converts to Islam before the wife takes possession. The question is whether the original specification of such impermissible items affects the mahr obligation after Islam. Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad each offer a distinct ruling based on differing analogies. Understanding their reasoning (talil) deepens your grasp of how Hanafi usul handles the interplay between contract and prohibited subject matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the specific case
Note the facts: dhimmi contract, mahr specified as wine or pork, conversion before qabd. Distinguish between mu'ayyan (specific item) and ghayr mu'ayyan (unspecified).
The ruling pivots on whether the dower was a specific bottle of wine / pig, or merely a description.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
For mu'ayyan, wife gets the wine/pork itself. For ghayr mu'ayyan, she gets value of wine, mahr al-mithl for pork. His illa: ownership of specific dower is perfected at contract; delivery only transfers guarantee.
He analogizes the recovery of usurped wine – Islam does not prevent taking possession of an owned item.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
He says mahr al-mithl in both cases. His illa: delivery affirms ownership like a contract; since Islam prohibits the item, the situation is as if the contract were made by Muslims.
He treats delivery as analogous to a fresh transaction that must comply with Islamic law.
4
State Muhammad's position
He says value (qimah) in both cases. His illa: the specification is valid (the item is property among dhimmis), but Islam prevents delivery; thus value is owed, as when a specified slave perishes before delivery.
He emphasizes that the impediment arises after a valid specification, so the wife is entitled to the monetary equivalent.
5
Compare the illa and the practical consequence
Notice how each imam's illa determines the outcome. Abu Hanifa focuses on the moment of contract; Abu Yusuf on delivery; Muhammad on the prevention. Also note the subsidiary ruling on divorce before consummation.
The thamarah al-khilaf: half vs mut'ah if divorced before consummation.

فان تزوج الذمي ذمية على خمر او خنزير ثم اسلما اواسلم احدهما فلها الخمر والخنزير ومعناه اذاكانا باعيانهما والإسلام قبل القبض و ان كانا بغير اعيانهما فلها في الخمر القيمة وفى الخنزير مهر المثل وهذا عند ابي حنيفة وقال ابويوسف لهامهر المثل فى الوجهين وقال محمد لها القيمة في الوجهين وجه قولهما ان القبض مؤكد للملك فى المقبوض فيكون له شبه بالعقد فيمتنع بسبب الاسلام كالعقد وصار كما اذاکانا بغيرا عيانهما واذا التحقت حالة القبض بحالة العقد فابويوسف يقول لو كانا مسلمين وقت العقد يجب مهر المثل فكذا ههنا و محمد يقول صحت التسمية لكون المسمى مالا عندهم الا انه امتنع التسليم للاسلام فتجب القيمة كما اذاهلك العبد المسمى قبل القبض و لابي حنيفة ان الملک فی الصداق المعين يتم بنفس العقد ولهذا تملك التصرف فيه وبالقبض ينتقل من ضمان الزوج الى ضمانها وذلك لا يمتنع بالاسلام كاسترداد الخمر المغصوب وفى غير المعين القبض موجب ملک العين فيمتنع بالاسلام بخلاف المشترى لان ملك التصرف انما يستفاد بالقبض واذا تعذر القبض فى غير المعين لاتجب القيمة في الخنزير لانه من ذوات القيم فيكون اخذ فيمته كاخذ عينه ولا كذلك الخمر لانها من ذوات الامثال الاترى انه لوجاء بالقيمة قبل الاسلام تجبر على القبول فى الخنزير دون الخمر و لو طلقها قبل الدخول بها . فمن اوجب مهر المثل اوجب المتعة و من اوجب القيمة اوجب نصفها
If a dhimmi man marries a dhimmi woman on [the condition of] wine or pork, then both become Muslim or one of them becomes Muslim, she is entitled to the wine and the pork. Its meaning: when they are specific items and the conversion is before delivery. If they are unspecified, then she is entitled to the value in the case of wine, and the mahr of the like in the case of pork. This is according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: she is entitled to the mahr of the like in both cases. Muhammad said: she is entitled to the value in both cases. The reasoning of the two (Sahibayn): delivery affirms ownership in the delivered item, so it resembles the contract; it becomes prohibited due to Islam like the contract, and it becomes as if they were unspecified. When the state of delivery attaches to the state of the contract, Abu Yusuf says: if both were Muslim at the time of the contract, the mahr of the like would be obligatory, so likewise here. Muhammad says: the specification is valid because the specified item is property according to them, but delivery is prevented due to Islam, so value is obligatory, as when the specified slave perishes before delivery. Abu Hanifa's proof: ownership of the specific dower is perfected by the contract itself; hence she owns disposal over it. By delivery, it transfers from the husband's guarantee to her guarantee, and this is not prevented by Islam, like the recovery of usurped wine. For unspecified items, delivery necessitates ownership of the specific object, so it is prevented by Islam, unlike the purchased item because the right to disposal is obtained only by delivery. When delivery is impossible for unspecified items, value is not obligatory in the case of pork because it is among items of value; taking its price is like taking its object. This is not so for wine because it is among items of measure. Do you not see that if he brings the value before Islam, she is forced to accept it in the case of pork but not in the case of wine? If he divorced her before consummation: whoever obligates the mahr of the like obligates mut'ah (gift of consolation), and whoever obligates the value obligates half of it.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the validity and valuation of mahr when a dhimmi husband marries a dhimmi wife with wine or pork as the dower, and then both or one convert to Islam before delivery. The core issue is whether the original specification of such non-fungible items (wine and pork) is affected by the conversion. For Abu Hanifa, if the items are specifically identified ('ayn mu'ayyan) at the time of the contract, ownership transfers immediately upon the contract itself, and conversion does not prevent the wife from taking possession of those items after conversion. Thus, she is entitled to the wine and pork themselves. However, if the items were unspecified (ghayr mu'ayyan), the wife receives the value of wine (since it is measured, value is recoverable) and the mahr of the like for pork (since it is a species not measured, it reverts to mahr al-mithl). Abu Yusuf considers that delivery is analogous to a new contract; since the items become unlawful upon Islam, the contract is voided and she gets mahr al-mithl in both cases. Imam Muhammad argues that since the items were validly specified (as they are property in the dhimmi's belief), but delivery is prevented by Islam, the wife is entitled to the value of the items in both cases. The passage also notes the consequence if divorce occurs before consummation: those who obligate mahr al-mithl give mut'ah (a consolation gift), while those who obligate value give half the value.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The dower given by the husband to the wife as a necessary consequence of the marriage contract.
معين (mu'ayyan): Specifically identified item such that it is distinguished from other items of its kind.
قبض (qabd): Physical or constructive taking possession of the dower by the wife.
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The like dower, i.e., the customary dower given to women of similar status.
قيمة (qimah): Monetary value, compensation for an item that is not fungible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a dhimmi husband marries a dhimmi woman with a specified (mu'ayyan) bottle of wine as mahr, and then both convert to Islam before delivery, what is the wife entitled to?
A) The wine itself
B) The value of the wine
C) Mahr al-mithl (the like dower)
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when dhimmi spouses convert, the original mahr of prohibited items is replaced by a permissible equivalent, but the imams differ on whether that equivalent is the item itself, mahr al-mithl, or value, based on the specification and timing of delivery.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
10 min
Mahr of Wine or Pig After Conversion: The Three Hanafi Positions
Explains the ruling when dhimmi spouses convert after mahr is wine/pig.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on mahr of wine/pig after conversion to Islam and apply the mufta bihi (Abu Hanifa's) ruling to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Ownership of specific mahr completes at contract; possession only shifts guarantee.

In this passage from Sharh al-Hidayah, we encounter a classic intra-Hanafi dispute about the fate of a mahr consisting of wine or pig when the spouse(s) convert to Islam before possession. Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad each propose different outcomes, revealing deeper principles about ownership, possession, and the effect of Islam on property rights. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply istidlal (legal reasoning) and to distinguish specific (mu'ayyan) from unspecified (ghayr mu'ayyan) obligations.

Guided Path
1
State the problem and the three positions
Identify the scenario: dhimmi spouses marry with mahr of wine or pig, then both or one convert to Islam before possession. Note that Abu Hanifa distinguishes between specific and unspecified mahr; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad each hold a universal rule.
Abu Hanifa's position is the mufta bihi in the school.
2
Trace the legal reasoning of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad)
Both Sahibayn argue that possession (qabd) confirms ownership, making it resemble the contract. Just as a new contract transferring wine/pig is prohibited after Islam, possession is also prohibited. They differ on the substitute: Abu Yusuf uses mahr al-mithl, Muhammad uses value (qima).
Their shared premise is that possession is convalidating and thus blocked by Islam.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's counterargument
Abu Hanifa holds that ownership of specific mahr is complete at the contract itself; possession merely transfers guarantee (damān). Since the contract was valid when made (as dhimmis), Islam does not undo existing ownership. For unspecified mahr, possession would create ownership of the substance, so it is blocked; hence a substitute is needed.
He uses the example of reclaiming stolen wine – also prohibited but permissible for the owner.
4
Determine the tarjih and mufta bihi
Al-Hidayah relies on Abu Hanifa's view as the zahir al-riwayah, and it is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the Hanafi school.
Tarjih authority: Al-Marghinani and later Hanafi masters.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a case (e.g., specific pig mahr, conversion before possession), state the outcome under each imam, then identify which is mufta bihi.
Test understanding of the distinctions between specific/unspecified and fungible/valuables.

ترجمہ پس اگر کسی ذمی نے کسی زمیہ سے نکاح کیا شراب پر یا خنزیر پر پھر دونوں مسلمان ہو گئے یا ان دونوں میں سے ایک مسلمان ہو گیا تو ذمیہ عورت کیلئے شراب اور خنزیر ہے اور امام محمد کے قول کے معنی یہ ہیں کہ جب وہ خمر اور خنزیر معین ہوں اور مسلمان ہو نا قبضہ سے پہلے ہو۔ اور اگر دونوں غیر معین ہیں تو زمیہ کیلئے شراب کی صورت میں قیمت ہے اور خنزیر میں مہر مثل ہے اور یہ ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک ہے۔ اور ابو یوسف نے فرمایا کہ زمینہ کیلئے دونوں صورتوں میں مہر مثل ہے اور امام محمد نے فرمایا کہ دونوں صورتوں میں قیمت ہے۔ اور صاحبین کے قول کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ قبضہ مقبوض میں ملک کیلئے مؤکد ہے۔ پس قبضہ عقد کے ساتھ مشابہ ہو گا۔ لہذا قبضہ عقد کی طرح اسلام کی وجہ سے ممتنع ہو گا۔ اور ایسا ہو گیا جیسا کہ جب دونوں غیر معین ہوں۔ اور جب قبضہ کی حالت عقد کی حالت کے ساتھ لاحق ہوگئی تو ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں که اگر دونوں عقد کے وقت مسلمان ہوتے تو مہر مثل واجب ہوتا۔ پس ایسا ہی یہاں۔ اور امام محمد فرماتے ہیں کہ تسمیہ صحیح ہے کیونکہ مسمی ان کے نزدیک مال ہے۔ مگر اسلام کی وجہ سے تسلیم سمی ممتنع ہو گیا۔ پس قیمت واجب ہوگی۔ جیسا کہ جب قبضہ سے پہلے عبد سمی ہلاک ہو جائے ۔ اور ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ ملک مہر معین میں نفس عقد کی وجہ سے پوری ہو جاتی ہے اور اسی وجہ سے عورت مہر معین میں تصرف کی مالک ہوگی۔ اور قبضہ سے مہر منتقل ہوتا ہے شوہر کے منمان سے عورت کے عمان کی طرف اور یہ انتقال اسلام کی وجہ سے ممتنع نہیں۔ جیسا کہ شراب مغصوب کو واپس لینا اور غیر معین میں قبضہ ملک عین کو واجب کرنے والا ہے۔ پس قبضہ اسلام کی وجہ سے ممتنع ہوگا۔ بخلاف مشترکی کے۔ اسلئے کہ ملک تصرف قبضہ سے مستفاد ہے۔ اور جب غیر معین میں قبضہ متعذر ہو گیا تو خنزیر میں قیمت واجب نہیں ہوگی۔ اسلئے کہ خنزیر ذوات القیم میں سے ہے۔ پس اس کی قیمت کو لینا ایسا ہے جیسا کہ عین خنزیر کو لینا۔ اور شراب ایسی نہیں ہے۔ اسلئے کہ شراب ذوات الامثال میں سے ہے۔ کیا نہیں دیکھتا تو کہ اگر شوہر قیمت ادا کر دے اسلام سے پہلے تو عورت کو خنزیر میں قبول کرنے پر مجبور کیا جائے گا نہ کہ شراب میں اور اگر طلاق دی عورت کو اس کے ساتھ دخول سے پہلے تو جس شخص نے مہر مثل واجب کیا وہ متعہ واجب کرے گا۔ اور جس نے قیمت واجب کی نصف قیمت واجب کرے گا۔

واجب ہوگی۔ جس طرح قبضہ سے پہلے اگر عبد سمٹی ہلاک ہو جائے تو اس کی قیمت واجب ہوتی ہے۔ اسی طرح یہاں بھی خمر اور خنزیر کی قیمت واجب ہوگی۔ امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ ملک مہر معین میں نفس عقد سے مکمل ہو جاتی ہے کیونکہ ملک کی دو قسمیں ہیں۔ ملک رقبہ اور دوم ملک تصرف ۔ اور عورت کیلئے قبضہ سے پہلے دونوں ثابت ہیں یہی وجہ ہے کہ عورت مہر معین پر قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے اس میں ہر طرح کے تصرف کی مالک ہے اور اگر قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے وہ مہر ہلاک ہو گیا تو وہ عورت کی ملک پر ہلاک ہوگا شوہر کی ملک پر نہیں۔ خلاصہ یہ کہ مہر معین میں قبضہ موجب ملک نہیں ہے۔ رہا یہ سوال کہ پھر قبضہ سے کیا فائدہ ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے جواب دیا کہ قبضہ کی وجہ سے ملک زوج کے ضمان سے بیوی کے عمان کی طرف منتقل ہو جاتی ہے اور یہ انتقال ملک اسلام کی وجہ سے ممنوع نہیں ہے۔ مثلاً ایک ذمی سے کسی نے اس کی شراب
If a dhimmi man marries a dhimmi woman with wine or pig (as mahr), then both become Muslim, or one of them becomes Muslim: She gets the wine and pig – meaning if they are specific (mu'ayyan) and Islam occurs before possession (qabd). If they are unspecified (ghayr mu'ayyan), then she gets the value (qima) for wine, and for pig, mahr al-mithl (proper dowry). This is according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: She gets mahr al-mithl in both cases. Muhammad said: She gets the value in both cases. The reasoning of the two (Sahibayn) is that possession (qabd) confirms ownership in the possessed object, so it resembles the contract; thus it becomes prohibited due to Islam like the contract, and it becomes as if they were unspecified. When the state of possession attaches to the state of contract, Abu Yusuf says: If both had been Muslims at the time of the contract, mahr al-mithl would be obligatory; so likewise here. Muhammad says: The specification (tasmiyya) is valid because the specified object is property in their religion, but delivery is prohibited due to Islam, so value is obligatory, just as if the specified slave perished before possession. Abu Hanifa's proof: Ownership in specific dower is complete by the contract itself; hence she has the right to dispose of it. Possession transfers it from the husband's guarantee to hers, and this transfer is not prevented by Islam, like reclaiming stolen wine. In unspecified (mahr), possession requires ownership of the substance, so it is prevented by Islam, unlike the case of purchase because the right of disposition is acquired by possession. When possession is impossible for unspecified, value is not obligatory for pig because it is among items of value (dhawat al-qiyam), so taking its value is like taking its substance. It is not so for wine because it is among items of measure (dhawat al-amthal). Do you not see that if he brought the value before Islam, she would be forced to accept it for pig but not for wine? If he divorces her before consummation, whoever obligates mahr al-mithl obligates compensation (mut'ah), and whoever obligates value obligates half of it. Translation: So if a dhimmi married a dhimmi with wine or pig, then both became Muslims, or one of them, then the dhimmi woman gets the wine and pig – meaning according to Muhammad's statement when they are specific and Islam occurs before possession. If both are unspecified, then the woman gets value for wine and mahr al-mithl for pig, according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: She gets mahr al-mithl in both cases. Muhammad said: She gets value in both cases. The reasoning of the two is that possession confirms ownership; so possession resembles the contract, thus it is prevented by Islam like the contract, and becomes as if unspecified. When the state of possession attaches to the state of contract, Abu Yusuf says: If both were Muslims at the time of the contract, mahr al-mithl would be obligatory; so likewise here. Muhammad says: The specification is valid because the specified object is property among them, but delivery is prevented by Islam, so value is obligatory, just as if the specified slave perished before possession. Abu Hanifa's proof: Ownership in specific dower is complete by the contract itself; for that reason she has the right to dispose of it. Possession transfers it from the husband's guarantee to hers, and this transfer is not prevented by Islam, like reclaiming stolen wine. In unspecified, possession requires ownership of the substance, so it is prevented by Islam, unlike the purchase because the right of disposition is only gained by possession. When possession becomes impossible for unspecified, value is not obligatory for pig because it is among items of value, so taking its value is like taking its substance. It is not so for wine because it is among items of measure. Do you not see that if the husband paid the value before Islam, the woman would be forced to accept it for pig but not for wine? And if he divorced her before consummation, whoever obligates mahr al-mithl obligates mut'ah, and whoever obligates value obligates half of it. Explanation of the case: A dhimmi man married a dhimmi woman and made the mahr specific wine or specific pig. Then before possession of the mahr, both or one of them became Muslim. In this case, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the dhimmi woman gets that specific wine and specific pig. If the mahr is unspecified wine or unspecified pig, then Imam Sahib says: For unspecified wine, the woman gets value; for unspecified pig, she gets mahr al-mithl. According to Imam Abu Yusuf, in both specific and unspecified, the woman gets mahr al-mithl. According to Imam Muhammad, in both specific and unspecified, the woman gets value. And since Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad both agree that the substance of wine and pig is not obligatory when they are specific, the author of Hidayah presented their combined proof. He said: The proof of the two is that possession confirms ownership in the specific possessed object. This is why if the specified mahr perishes before the woman takes possession, it perishes from the husband's property, and he owes its equivalent if it was among items of measure, or its value if among items of value. If it perishes after possession, it perishes from the woman's property. Similarly, according to Imam Sahib, before possession zakat on the mahr is not obligatory on the woman, but after possession it is. So when possession confirms ownership, possession resembles the contract because both affect ownership. Just as initiating a contract to transfer wine or pig is prohibited after Islam, similarly taking possession of wine or pig after Islam is prohibited. This is like unspecified wine and pig – just as the substance is not obligatory for unspecified, so too for specific. When the state of possession resembles the state of contract and delivery of the substance of wine and pig becomes prohibited, Imam Abu Yusuf says: If they had been Muslims at the time of the contract on wine and pig, mahr al-mithl would be obligatory; therefore when they are Muslim at the time of possession, mahr al-mithl is still obligatory. Imam Muhammad says: The specification of wine and pig is valid because among dhimmis the specified object is property. But since delivery of wine and pig is prohibited due to Islam, their value is obligatory, just as if the specified slave perishes before possession, its value is obligatory. Similarly here, the value of wine and pig is obligatory. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof: Ownership in specific mahr is complete by the contract itself because ownership has two types: ownership of the substance and ownership of disposition. For the woman, both are established before possession. This is why the woman has the right to all types of disposition in the specific mahr before taking possession, and if the mahr perishes before possession, it perishes from her ownership, not the husband's. The conclusion is that in specific mahr, possession does not cause ownership. As for the question: What benefit does possession have? The author of Hidayah answered: Possession transfers ownership from the husband's guarantee to the wife's trust, and this transfer of ownership is not prevented by Islam. For example, if someone took wine from a dhimmi...
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Hidayah presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute concerning mahr (dower) when it consists of wine or pig, items that are ritually impure (najis) and non-property (ghayr mal) for Muslims but property for dhimmis. The case involves a marriage contract between dhimmis where the mahr is wine or pig, followed by conversion to Islam of both or one spouse before possession (qabd) of the mahr. The key issue is whether the woman is entitled to the substance of the wine/pig, its value (qima), or the proper dower (mahr al-mithl). Three positions emerge: Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes between specific (mu'ayyan) and unspecified (ghayr mu'ayyan) mahr. For specific, the substance is due because ownership is complete by the contract alone; possession merely shifts liability. For unspecified, wine requires value (since it is fungible, min dhawat al-amthal) and pig requires mahr al-mithl (since pig is a valuables item, min dhawat al-qiyam, taking its value is like taking its substance). Imam Abu Yusuf says mahr al-mithl in both cases, arguing that possession, like the contract, is prohibited after Islam, so the woman gets neither the substance nor its value but instead a proper dower. Imam Muhammad says value in both cases, because the specification is valid (the items are property for dhimmis) but delivery is prohibited after Islam, so value substitutes. The passage then details each imam's reasoning: the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) agree that specific mahr cannot be delivered after Islam, but they differ on the substitute; Abu Hanifa relies on the principle that ownership of specific dower is complete at the contract, so Islam cannot undo it. The commentary further explains that the Sahibayn's combined proof focuses on possession being a confirmatory cause (mu'akkid) of ownership, equating it with the contract in terms of preventing transfer after Islam. Abu Hanifa counters that possession merely transfers guarantee, not ownership itself. The practical consequence: if divorce occurs before consummation, half of what is due (mahr al-mithl or half value) applies. This dispute illustrates Hanafi method in balancing textual sources (hadith on mahr) and legal reasoning (qiyas, istihsan).

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The dower given by the husband to the wife in marriage; in this passage, it is wine or pig.
قبض (qabd): Possession or taking delivery; the point after which liability transfers.
معين (mu'ayyan): Specific, particularized (e.g., a specific bottle of wine or a specific pig).
غير معين (ghayr mu'ayyan): Unspecified, generic (e.g., wine in general or pig in general).
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): A proper dower equal to that of women of similar status; used as substitute when the specified mahr is invalid.
قيمة (qima): Monetary value or compensation; for fungible goods (like wine) value is market price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what does the dhimmi woman receive if the mahr is unspecified wine and both spouses convert to Islam before possession?
A) The specified wine itself
B) The value (qima) of the wine
C) Mahr al-mithl
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, ownership of specific mahr is complete at the contract; conversion to Islam does not invalidate existing ownership, but possession may be prevented, requiring a substitute for unspecified mahr.

Nikah — Marriage
advanced
10 min
Mahr of Wine or Pork in Dhimmi Marriage upon Conversion: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mahr validity when dhimmis convert before possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of the three Hanafi imams on the validity of mahr consisting of wine or pig in a dhimmi marriage when the parties convert to Islam before possession, and apply the reasoning regarding ownership and possession to determine the correct ruling.

Core Idea
In a dhimmi marriage, specified mahr of wine/pig remains valid upon conversion according to Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn differ based on the role of possession.

This lesson explores a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a dhimmi couple who marry with a dower of wine or pig, then convert to Islam before taking possession of that dower. The three eponymous imams – Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani – each derive different rulings based on their understanding of ownership and possession in mahr. You will analyze their arguments and the practical consequences of each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the key variables
State the facts: dhimmi marriage, mahr consists of wine (khamr) or pig (khinzir), either specified (mu'ayyan) or unspecified (ghayr mu'ayyan), conversion of one or both spouses before possession (qabd).
Note the distinction between specified and unspecified mahr – it is central to the dispute.
2
State the positions of the three imams
List Abu Hanifa's view: specified mahr valid; unspecified: wine → value, pig → mahr mithl. Abu Yusuf: mahr mithl in all cases. Muhammad: value in all cases.
These positions apply only to the scenario before possession.
3
Trace the reasoning of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf & Muhammad)
Understand why they invalidate the specific wine/pig: possession strengthens ownership and is analogized to the contract itself; since delivery after conversion is prohibited, the contract fails and a substitute is due. Abu Yusuf uses the analogy to the original contract (mahr mithl), Muhammad analogizes to a slave perishing before delivery (value).
Their joint proof is that possession (qabd) 'strengthens' ownership, making it akin to the initial contract.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's counter-argument
Abu Hanifa distinguishes ownership of substance (milk al-raqabah) from ownership of disposal (milk al-tasarruf). In specified mahr, both are complete by the contract alone – possession only transfers liability. Therefore, the prohibition after conversion does not affect already-complete ownership. For unspecified mahr, possession is needed for ownership, so substitution is required.
Key example: a usurped wine taken back after conversion is permissible – showing that possession of the specific item is not prohibited if ownership was already established.
5
Apply to divorce before consummation
If the husband divorces before consummation, the dispute continues: whoever holds mahr mithl as the substitute gives half of mahr mithl (mut'ah)? Actually, the text says: whoever made mahr mithl wajib gives mut'ah; whoever made price wajib gives half price. Trace this difference.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) appears in divorce cases as well.

کیلئے قبضہ کرنا جائز نہیں ہے اور اگر بكسر الراء ہے تو مطلب یہ ہوگا کہ مہر معین میں عورت یلئے نفس عقد کی وجہ سے ملک مکمل ہو جاتی ہے۔ بخلاف مشتری کے کیونکہ مشتری کیلئے نفس بیع کی وجہ سے ملک مکمل نہیں ہوتی ہے کیونکہ قبضہ سے اس کیلئے ملک تصرف مستفاد ہوگی ۔ امام ابو حنیفہ فرماتے ہیں کہ جب مہر غیر معین میں اسلام کی وجہ سے قبضہ متعذر ہو گیا تو خنزیر کی صورت میں شوہر پر اس کی قیمت واجب نہیں ہوگی بلکہ مہر مثل واجب ہوگا۔ کیونکہ خنزیر ذوات القیم میں سے ہے۔ لہذا اس کی قیمت کا لینا عین خنزیر کا لینا ہوگا۔ اور یہ درست نہیں ہے۔ اسلئے اس صورت میں مہر مثل واجب ہوگا۔ اور شراب چونکہ ذوات الامثال میں سے ہے اسلئے اس کی قیمت کو لینا عین شراب کو لینا نہیں ہو گا۔ اس وجہ سے شراب کی صورت میں عورت کیلئے شراب کی قیمت واجب کی گئی ہے اور چونکہ خنز سر ذوات القیم میں سے ہے اسی وجہ سے اگر اسلام سے پہلے شوہر خنزیر کی قیمت دینا چاہے تو عورت کو اسکے لینے پر مجبور کیا جائے گا۔ البتہ شراب کی صورت میں مجبور نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ شراب ذوات القیم میں سے نہیں ہے۔ صاحب بدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ مذکورہ بالا صورت میں اگر شوہر نے طلاق قبل الدخول دے دی تو جس نے مہر مثل واجب کیا تھا اس کے نزدیک متعہ واجب ہوگا اور جس نے قیمت واجب کی تھی اس کے نزدیک نصف قیمت واجب ہو گی ۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب
From the category of items of value. So taking its price is like taking the actual pig. And wine is not like that, because wine is from items of like nature. Do you not see that if the husband paid the price before Islam, the woman would be forced to accept the pig, not the wine. And if he divorced her before consummation, then whoever made the mahr mithl obligatory would make mut'ah obligatory, and whoever made the price obligatory would make half the price obligatory. Explanation: A dhimmi man married a dhimmi woman and made the mahr specified wine or specified pig. Then before taking possession, both converted to Islam, or one of them converted. In that case, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, for the dhimmi woman the specified wine and specified pig are valid. If the mahr is unspecified wine or unspecified pig, Imam Abu Hanifa says: for unspecified wine, the woman is entitled to its price; for unspecified pig, she is entitled to mahr mithl. According to Abu Yusuf, in both specified and unspecified cases, the woman is entitled to mahr mithl. According to Muhammad, in both specified and unspecified cases, the woman is entitled to the price. Because Abu Yusuf and Muhammad both agree that the specific wine and specific pig are not wajib when they are specified. So the author of Hidayah gave their joint proof: possession in specified items strengthens ownership. This is why if the mahr perishes before possession, it perishes from the husband's property, and he must give its like if it was from items of like nature, or its price if from items of value. If it perishes after possession, it perishes from the woman's property. Similarly, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, before possession zakat is not obligatory on the woman for the mahr, but after possession it is. So when possession strengthens ownership, it resembles the contract, because both affect ownership. Just as after Islam, transferring wine and pig via contract is prohibited, similarly after Islam, possession of wine and pig is prohibited. This is like unspecified wine and pig: just as in unspecified, the specific wine and pig are not wajib, so in specified as well, the specific wine and pig are not wajib. When the state of possession resembles the state of contract, and handing over the specific wine and pig becomes impossible, Abu Yusuf says: if they had been Muslim at the time of the contract, mahr mithl would be wajib; so now that they are Muslim at the time of possession, mahr mithl is also wajib. Muhammad says: the specification of wine and pig is valid because for dhimmis it is property, but since delivering them is prohibited due to Islam, their price becomes wajib, just as if a specified slave perishes before possession, his price is wajib. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof: ownership in specified mahr is complete by the contract itself, because there are two types of ownership: ownership of the substance and ownership of disposal. Before possession, the woman has both; she can dispose of it in any way. If it perishes before possession, it perishes from her ownership, not the husband's. So in specified mahr, possession does not cause ownership. As for the benefit of possession: due to possession, the ownership transfers from the husband's liability to the wife's guarantee, and this transfer is not prohibited by Islam. For example, if someone usurped a dhimmi's wine and then the dhimmi became Muslim, it is permissible for the now-Muslim to take back that wine from the usurper. In unspecified mahr, possession does cause ownership, and both transferring and possessing wine and pig are prohibited due to Islam, so possession is also prohibited. Unlike a purchase: in specified mahr, ownership is complete by the contract itself, unlike in a sale where ownership of disposal is achieved by possession, not by the contract itself. For example, if someone sold or bought wine or pig, and then before possession the buyer became Muslim, it is not permissible for him to take possession. Imam Abu Hanifa says: when possession becomes impossible in unspecified mahr due to Islam, then for pig, the price is not wajib because pig is from items of value; taking its price is like taking the actual pig, which is not valid, so mahr mithl is wajib. For wine, since it is from items of like nature, taking its price is not like taking actual wine, so the price is wajib. Because pig is from items of value, if the husband wants to pay its price before Islam, the woman is forced to accept; for wine, she is not forced. The author of Bidayah says: in the above case, if the husband divorces before consummation, then whoever made mahr mithl wajib makes mut'ah wajib, and whoever made price wajib makes half price wajib. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the validity of mahr (dower) when it consists of wine (khamr) or pig (khinzir) in a marriage between dhimmis (non-Muslim subjects) who subsequently convert to Islam before taking possession of the mahr. The dispute centers on whether the mahr remains valid as specified (mu'ayyan) or must be substituted with mahr mithl (equivalent dower) or value (qimah). The text presents three positions. Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes between specified and unspecified mahr: if the wine or pig is specifically identified, the mahr is valid and the woman takes it upon conversion; but if unspecified, then for wine (which is from items of like nature, dhawat al-amthal) she takes its value, while for pig (which is from items of value, dhawat al-qim) she takes mahr mithl. Imam Abu Yusuf holds that in both cases, the woman gets mahr mithl because possession strengthens ownership and is analogous to the contract; since delivering wine and pig is prohibited after conversion, the contract is effectively voided. Imam Muhammad holds that specification is valid, but delivery is impossible; therefore, the value (qimah) is payable in all cases, analogizing to the case of a specified slave that perishes before delivery. The reasoning involves a detailed comparison of ownership in mahr versus sale, and the effect of possession. The text also discusses the consequence if the husband divorces before consummation: the dispute continues regarding whether mut'ah (consolatory gift) or half the value is due. This passage demonstrates the Hanafi school's nuanced approach to combining contract validity with post-contract impediments, using legal analogy (qiyas) and considering the nature of the subject matter (like vs. value). The positions are cited from Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, and are explained in the Hidayah commentary.

Key Terms
مهر معين (mahr mu'ayyan): A dower that is specifically identified (e.g., this particular bottle of wine or this pig).
مهر غير معين (mahr ghayr mu'ayyan): A dower that is not specifically identified but described in generic terms (e.g., a certain quantity of wine).
ذوات الامثال (dhawat al-amthal): Items that are sold by like measure or weight (e.g., wine measured by volume), such that compensation is by like amount.
ذوات القيم (dhawat al-qim): Items that vary in value individually (e.g., pigs, land), such that compensation is by market price.
قبضه (qabdah): Taking physical possession of the mahr after the contract.
ملك رقبه (milk al-raqabah): Ownership of the substance of an item, giving the right to dispose of it (sell, gift, use) as owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling for an unspecified mahr of wine in a dhimmi marriage where the couple converts to Islam before possession?
A) The woman gets the specified wine itself.
B) The woman gets the value (qimah) of the wine.
C) The woman gets mahr mithl (equivalent dower).
D) The marriage is invalid and no mahr is due.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of mahr in dhimmi marriages upon conversion depends on whether the mahr was specified or unspecified, and the imams differ on the effect of possession, leading to three distinct positions.

Marriage of Slave Requires Master's Permission
Ruling with proof and Maliki opposition on slave marriage validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on slave marriage, cite the hadith proof, and explain the Maliki counter-argument and its rebuttal.

Core Idea
A slave's marriage without the master's permission is invalid; the hadith declares it fornication.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents the ruling that male and female slaves cannot marry without their master's permission. The Hanafi school relies on a specific hadith to support this ruling, while Imam Malik disagrees based on the slave's capacity for divorce. This lesson unpacks the proof and the dispute.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What exactly is the Hanafi ruling on a slave marrying without the master's permission? Identify the act and its legal classification.
The text says 'لا يجوز' (not permissible) and the hadith calls such a slave a 'fornicator'.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Which school disagrees? What is their ruling and on what basis?
Imam Malik permits the slave to marry because the slave has the right to divorce.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What evidence does the Maliki school use? Analyze their reasoning.
They argue that the right to divorce implies the right to marry (ownership of talaq implies ownership of nikah).
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school rebut the opponent's argument? What is the primary dalil?
The primary dalil is the hadith; additionally, marriage is a 'defect' (ayb) for the slave's service to the master.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference in rulings between the two schools?
Hanafi: marriage is batil (void). Maliki: marriage is valid but the master can object.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب نكاح الرقيق

ترجمہ یہ باب رقیق کے نکاح کے بیان میں ہے۔

تشریح نکاح رفیق کو نصرانی اور نصرانیہ کی فصل سے مؤخر کیا ہے اسلئے کہ رق آثار کفر سے ہے ۔ کیونکہ ابتداء کا فرکور قیق بنایا جاتا ہے مسلمان کو نہیں اور چونکہ اثر مؤثر کے بعد آتا ہے اسلئے نکاح رقیق کو بعد میں بیان کیا ۔ رقیق مملوک کو کہتے ہیں۔ رقیق واحد جمع سب پر بولا جاتا ہے۔

غلام اور باندی کا نکاح آقا کی اجازت سے جائز ہے

لا يجوزنكاح العبدوالامة الاباذن مولاهما وقال مالک یجوز للعبدلانه یملک الطلاق فیملک النکاح ولنا قوله عليه السلام ايما عبد تزوج بغير اذن مولاه فهو عاهر ولان فى تنفيذنكا حهما تعييبهما اذا النكاح عيب فيهما فلا يملكانه بدون اذن مولاهما

ترجمہ غلام اور باندی کا نکاح جائز نہیں مگر ان دونوں کے مولی کی اجازت کے ساتھ اور امام مالک نے فرمایا کہ غلام کیلئے جائز ہے
Chapter on the marriage of slaves.
Translation: This chapter is about the marriage of slaves.
Explanation: The marriage of slaves is placed after the section on Christians because slavery is a consequence of disbelief, since initially disbelief enslaves, not a Muslim. And because the effect comes after the cause, the marriage of slaves is mentioned later. 'Raqiq' means a slave. 'Raqiq' is used for singular and plural.

The marriage of a male slave and a female slave is not permissible except with the permission of their master. Malik said: It is permissible for a male slave because he has the right of divorce, so he has the right of marriage. And for us (Hanafis), there is the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Any male slave who marries without the permission of his master is a fornicator.' And also because executing their marriage would cause a defect for them, since marriage is a defect for them, so they do not have the right to marry without their master's permission.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage introduces a new chapter on the marriage of slaves (raqiq). The author first explains the placement: since slavery is an effect of disbelief (kufr), and the cause (disbelief) precedes the effect, the topic of slave marriage comes after the discussion of marriages of non-Muslims (the previous chapter). The term 'raqiq' applies to both male and female slaves. The central ruling is stated: a male slave (abd) and a female slave (amah) cannot marry without the permission of their respective masters. The proof is twofold: a hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) that any slave who marries without the master's permission is a fornicator (meaning the marriage is invalid and it is considered illegal intercourse), and a rational argument that marriage would be a defect (ayb) for the slave because it draws the slave away from the master's service, so without the master's consent, the slave cannot undertake a marriage that could harm the master's interests. The Maliki view is presented: Imam Malik allows the male slave to marry independently because the slave has the right to divorce (talaq), and having the right to divorce implies having the right to marry. The Hanafis argue that the right to divorce is separate; the slave's ownership of divorce does not grant independent marriage authority because the master's rights override. The text then provides the translation into Urdu.

Key Terms
رقيق (raqiq): A male or female slave (singular and plural used interchangeably).
عاهر (ahir): Fornicator, indicating the marriage is invalid and the intercourse is sinful.
عيب (ayb): Defect, referring to marriage being a disadvantage for the slave's service to the master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence for the Hanafi ruling that a slave cannot marry without the master's permission?
A) Consensus (ijma) of the Companions.
B) The slave lacks the legal capacity for divorce.
C) The hadith: 'Any slave who marries without his master's permission is a fornicator.'
D) The slave's marriage would harm the master's property rights.

Key Takeaway
A slave's marriage without the master's permission is invalid (batil) and the hadith directly proves this.

Distinguishing Wine and Pig in Mahr: The Hanafi Rule on Conversion
Ruling on mahr when specified item becomes impermissible due to Islam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between dhawat al-amthal and dhawat al-qeem and apply the Hanafi rule for mahr when the specified item becomes impermissible due to conversion to Islam.

Core Idea
Wine's value can be taken; pig's value cannot — the legal category determines the dower.

The Hanafi school treats wine and pig differently when they are specified as mahr (dower) and the husband converts to Islam. The ruling hinges on whether the item is fungible (dhawat al-amthal) or non-fungible (dhawat al-qeem). This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning and practical consequences, including the effect of divorce before consummation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Problem
When a husband converts to Islam after specifying wine or pig as mahr, he cannot lawfully deliver the item. What becomes of the dower obligation?
The answer depends on the type of item: fungible or non-fungible.
2
Classify the Item as Dhawat al-Amthal or Dhawat al-Qeem
Wine is fungible (dhawat al-amthal) — it can be replaced by similar units. Pig is non-fungible (dhawat al-qeem) — its value is assessed individually.
This classification is central to the Hanafi reasoning.
3
Apply the Hanafi Rule
For fungible wine: the wife is entitled to its value (qimah). For non-fungible pig: taking its value is like taking the pig itself — impermissible — so the wife receives mahr al-mithl (dower of her peers).
This rule is from Imam Abu Hanifa (rahimahullah).
4
Consider the Underlying Principle
The rationale (ta'lil) is that for dhawat al-qeem, value substitutes for the specific item, so paying value is effectively giving the pig. For dhawat al-amthal, value is a separate compensation.
This is a form of qiyas (analogical reasoning).
5
Examine the Effect of Divorce Before Consummation
If the husband divorces before consummation, the owed amount changes. According to the view that mahr al-mithl is due (for pig), only mut'ah (gift) is obligatory. According to the view that value is due (for wine), half the value is obligatory.
This ikhtilaf is cited from the author of al-Bidayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۱۵۰
کتاب النکاح
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد چہارم
کو غصب کر لیا اس کے بعد ذمی مسلمان ہو گیا تو اب اس ذمی مسلمان کیلئے غاصب سے اس شراب مغصوبہ کو لینا جائز ہے۔ اور مہر غیر معین میں قبضہ میں موجب ملک ہے اور اسلام کی وجہ سے تملیک خمر اور تملیک خمر دونوں ممتنع ہیں۔ لہذا قبضہ بھی ممتنع ہوگا۔ بخلاف المشترى كالعتق ان الملک فی الصداق المعین الخ سے ہے مشتری بفتح الراء اور بكسر الراء دونوں ہو سکتا ہے۔ پہلی صورت میں مبیع مراد ہوگی۔ اور مطلب یہ ہوگا کہ مہر معین میں ملک نفس عقد سے کامل ہو جاتی ہے۔ بخلاف بیع کے، کیونکہ بیع میں قبضہ کی وجہ سے ملک تصرف حاصل ہوتی ہے نفس بیع کی وجہ سے نہیں۔ مثلاً ایک شخص نے شراب یا خنزیر فروخت کیا یا خریدا پھر قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے مشتری (خریدار ) اسلام لے آیا تو اس کیلئے قبضہ کرنا جائز نہیں ہے اور اگر بكسر الراء ہے تو مطلب یہ ہوگا کہ مہر معین میں عورت یلئے نفس عقد کی وجہ سے ملک مکمل ہو جاتی ہے۔ بخلاف مشتری کے کیونکہ مشتری کیلئے نفس بیع کی وجہ سے ملک مکمل نہیں ہوتی ہے کیونکہ قبضہ سے اس کیلئے ملک تصرف مستفاد ہوگی ۔
امام ابو حنیفہ فرماتے ہیں کہ جب مہر غیر معین میں اسلام کی وجہ سے قبضہ متعذر ہو گیا تو خنزیر کی صورت میں شوہر پر اس کی قیمت واجب نہیں ہوگی بلکہ مہر مثل واجب ہوگا۔ کیونکہ خنزیر ذوات القیم میں سے ہے۔ لہذا اس کی قیمت کا لینا عین خنزیر کا لینا ہوگا۔ اور یہ درست نہیں ہے۔ اسلئے اس صورت میں مہر مثل واجب ہوگا۔ اور شراب چونکہ ذوات الامثال میں سے ہے اسلئے اس کی قیمت کو لینا عین شراب کو لینا نہیں ہو گا۔ اس وجہ سے شراب کی صورت میں عورت کیلئے شراب کی قیمت واجب کی گئی ہے اور چونکہ خنز سر ذوات القیم میں سے ہے اسی وجہ سے اگر اسلام سے پہلے شوہر خنزیر کی قیمت دینا چاہے تو عورت کو اسکے لینے پر مجبور کیا جائے گا۔ البتہ شراب کی صورت میں مجبور نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ شراب ذوات القیم میں سے نہیں ہے۔ صاحب بدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ مذکورہ بالا صورت میں اگر شوہر نے طلاق قبل الدخول دے دی تو جس نے مہر مثل واجب کیا تھا اس کے نزدیک متعہ واجب ہوگا اور جس نے قیمت واجب کی تھی اس کے نزدیک نصف قیمت واجب ہو گی ۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب
He usurped it, then the dhimmi became Muslim. So now it is permissible for this dhimmi Muslim to take that usurped wine from the usurper. And in unspecified mahr, possession is a cause of ownership, and due to Islam, the ownership of wine and pig are both prohibited. Therefore, possession is also prohibited. Contrary to the buyer, like emancipation, that ownership in specified dower is complete from the contract itself. From 'al-mushtara' with fatha on ra and kasra on ra, both are possible. In the first case, the sold item is intended, and the meaning will be that in specified dower, ownership becomes complete from the contract itself, contrary to a sale, because in a sale, ownership of disposal is obtained by possession, not by the contract itself. For example, a person sold or bought wine or pig, then before possession, the buyer became Muslim, then for him taking possession is not permissible. And if with kasra on ra, the meaning will be that in specified dower, the woman gets complete ownership due to the contract itself, contrary to the buyer, because for the buyer, ownership is not complete from the contract itself, but from possession he obtains ownership of disposal. Imam Abu Hanifa says: When in unspecified mahr, possession becomes impossible due to Islam, then in the case of pig, the value will not be obligatory on the husband, but mahr al-mithl will be obligatory, because pig is among items of value. Therefore, taking its value would be like taking the pig itself, and that is not correct. Hence, in this case, mahr al-mithl is obligatory. And as for wine, since it is among items of measure, taking its value is not like taking the wine itself. Therefore, in the case of wine, the woman is made entitled to the value of the wine. And since pig is among items of value, for that reason, if before Islam the husband wants to pay the value of the pig, the woman will be forced to take it. However, in the case of wine, she will not be forced, because wine is not among items of value. The author of al-Bidayah says: In the above-mentioned case, if the husband gave divorce before consummation, then according to the one who made mahr al-mithl obligatory, the mut'ah will be obligatory, and according to the one who made the value obligatory, half the value will be obligatory. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on mahr (dower) when the specified item is unlawful (wine or pig) and the husband converts to Islam. The author distinguishes between specified and unspecified mahr. In specified mahr, ownership is completed by the contract itself, unlike a sale where possession is needed. When a Muslim husband cannot lawfully possess wine or pig as mahr, the ruling differs based on legal categories: wine (khamr) is from 'dhawat al-amthal' (fungible goods) so its value can be taken; pig (khinzir) is from 'dhawat al-qeem' (valuable goods) so taking its value is like taking the pig itself, which is impermissible. Hence, for pig, mahr al-mithl (dower of the like) is obligatory. The author also cites the position of the author of al-Bidayah on the effect of divorce before consummation in such cases.

Key Terms
ذوات القیم (dhawat al-qeem): Items that are not fungible; their value is assessed individually, e.g., pig.
ذوات الامثال (dhawat al-amthal): Fungible goods that can be replaced by similar items, e.g., wine, wheat.
مہر مثل (mahr al-mithl): Dower equivalent to that of women of similar status in the family.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a specified mahr is a pig and the husband converts to Islam before delivery, what becomes obligatory?
A) The pig itself
B) The value of the pig
C) Mahr al-mithl (dower of the like)
D) Half the value

Key Takeaway
Compensation for an impermissible mahr item depends on its legal category: value for fungibles (dhawat al-amthal), mahr al-mithl for non-fungibles (dhawat al-qeem).

Nikah — Marriage

Marriage of Slaves Requires Master's Permission: Hanafi-Maliki Dispute
Ruling that slaves cannot marry without master's permission, with proof and response to Malik.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on a slave's marriage without permission, state the hadith proof, and evaluate the Maliki counter-argument using the distinction between removal and creation of a defect.

Core Idea
A slave's marriage without permission is valid but suspended; it becomes effective only with the master's consent.

This passage addresses the legal capacity of slaves to contract marriage. The Hanafi school holds that a male or female slave cannot marry without the master's permission, based on an explicit hadith that such a marriage makes the slave a fornicator and on the rationale that marriage is a defect in the slave. Imam Malik argues that since the slave owns divorce, he also owns marriage by analogy. The lesson unpacks these proofs and the Hanafi rebuttal, clarifying that the marriage is valid (sahih) but its effect (nafadh) is suspended upon the master's permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the opening Arabic sentence: 'لا يجوز نكاح العبد والأمة إلا بإذن مولاهما'. Determine the Hanafi ruling and note that 'لا يجوز' means it is not permissible without permission.
The text explains that the correct interpretation is that the marriage is valid but not effective (mawquf), not batil.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Examine the hadith from Jabir: 'Any slave who marries without his master's permission is a fornicator.' Understand how this hadith directly links the act to zina, indicating prohibition. Also consider the rational proof: marriage is a defect in a slave, and a slave cannot create a defect without the master's consent.
The defect rationale is supported by the buyer's right to return a married slave due to defect.
3
Evaluate the Maliki counter-argument
Read Imam Malik's argument: the slave owns divorce, so he must also own marriage (since divorce is the removal of the marriage contract). Understand the qiyas: whoever owns the removal of a thing owns its establishment.
This is a qiyas jaliy based on the cause-effect relationship.
4
Study the Hanafi response
The response distinguishes between removal and creation: divorce is removal of a defect (marriage is a defect), while marriage is creation of a defect. Ownership of removal does not imply ownership of creation.
This rebuttal preserves the hadith as the primary proof.
5
Apply the ruling to other categories
Consider the extension to mukatab, mudabbar, and umm al-walad. The text states that all require permission because ownership remains partially. Understand why a mukatab can marry off his female slave (earning) but not himself or his male slave.
The principle: nikah is not an act of earning for the slave.
