وکذلك المكاتب لان الكتابة او جبت فك الحجر في حق الكسب فبقى في حق النكاح على حكم الرق ولهذا لايملك المكاتب تزويج عبده ويملك تزويج امته لانه من باب الاكتساب وكذا المكاتبة لا تملک تزویج نفسها بدون اذن المولى و تملک تزویج امتها لما بينا وكذا المدبر وام الولدلان الملك فيهما قائم
Chapter: Marriage of slaves
Translation: This chapter is concerning the marriage of a slave.
Explanation: The chapter on marriage of a slave has been postponed after the section on Christians and Christian women because slavery is an effect of disbelief. Initially, disbelief makes one a slave, not a Muslim. And since the effect comes after the cause, the marriage of slaves is mentioned later. A 'raqiq' is called a slave (mamlook). 'Raqiq' is used for singular, plural, and all.
The marriage of a male slave and a female slave is permissible only with permission of their master.
Malik said: It is permissible for a male slave because he owns divorce, so he owns marriage. Our proof is the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Any slave who marries without the permission of his master is a fornicator.' And because executing their marriage would be to make them defective, as marriage is a defect in them. So they do not own it without their master's permission.
Translation: The marriage of a male slave and a female slave is not permissible except with the permission of their master. Imam Malik said that it is permissible for a male slave because the slave owns divorce, therefore he also owns marriage. And our proof is the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): Any slave who marries without his master's permission is a fornicator. And because in implementing their marriage, they are made defective, since marriage is a defect in them. So they do not own it without their master's permission.
Explanation: One meaning of 'la yajuz' is that the marriage of a slave without master's permission does not contract at all. Hafiz al-Din holds this. But the correct meaning is that it is permissible and valid, but not effective. Its effectiveness is suspended upon the master's permission. If the master gives permission, it becomes effective; if he does not, it does not. This is the saying of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, Hasan, and Ibrahim al-Makhfi.
Conclusion: The marriage of a slave-girl without master's permission is by consensus not effective. Because the benefits of the slave-girl's vulva belong to the master, and no one has the right to dispose of the master's property without his permission. Therefore, without the master's permission, the slave-girl's marriage is not correct (effective). As for the male slave, there is disagreement with Imam Malik: he says that without master's permission, the male slave's marriage is permissible (effective). According to the Hanafis, the male slave's marriage without master's permission is not permissible (effective). Imam Malik's proof is that the slave owns divorce, and whoever owns divorce also owns marriage, because divorce is due to marriage, and whoever owns a thing also owns its cause that leads to it. Therefore, since the slave owns divorce, he also owns its cause (marriage). This proof can also be stated as: the slave owns divorce, and divorce is the removal of marriage, and whoever owns the removal of a thing also owns the establishment of that thing. Therefore, the slave also owns marriage. The Hanafi proof is the hadith of Jabir: 'Any slave who marries without his master's permission is a fornicator.' This hadith indicates that without the master's permission, the marriage is not valid (effective). The rational proof is that in making their marriage effective, they are made defective, because marriage is a defect in slaves. (That is why if a person buys a male or female slave and then discovers that he/she is married, the buyer has the right to return them due to the defect of marriage.) And the slave does not have the authority to create a defect in himself while observing the right of the master. So both of them do not own marriage without their master's permission. The response to Imam Malik's proof is that divorce is removal of a defect, while marriage is making defective. Ownership of removal of a defect does not imply ownership of creating a defect.
The marriage of a mukatab is also valid with the master's permission.
And likewise the mukatab, because kitabah necessitated the removal of interdiction regarding earning, so he remains under the ruling of slavery regarding marriage. Hence, the mukatab does not own the right to marry off his male slave, but he does own the right to marry off his female slave because that is a form of earning. And likewise, the mukatabah does not own the right to marry herself without the master's permission, but she owns the right to marry off her female slave, as we have explained. And likewise, the mudabbar and umm al-walad, because ownership remains in them.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This section from the chapter on marriage of slaves presents the Hanafi ruling that a slave (male or female) cannot contract a valid marriage without the master's permission. The text begins by explaining why this chapter is placed after the section on Christians—since slavery stems from disbelief. Then it presents the core ruling in Arabic with translation. The Hanafis base their position on the hadith of Jabir that such a marriage makes the slave a fornicator, and on rational argument: marriage in a slave is a defect (since a married slave is less valuable, giving the buyer an option of defect). Since a slave cannot create a defect in himself without the master's leave, he lacks capacity. Imam Malik argues that since the slave owns divorce, he must own marriage (the cause). The Hanafi response distinguishes divorce as removal of a defect from marriage as creation of a defect; owning removal does not imply owning creation. The text also clarifies that the marriage without permission is valid but suspended (mawquf) upon the master's ratification. Finally, it extends the same ruling to other categories: mukatab, mudabbar, umm al-walad—all require permission because ownership over them remains to some extent.

Key Terms
رقیق (raqeeq): A slave, male or female; used for both singular and plural.
عارہر (aaher): Fornicator; one who commits zina.
نكاح موقوف (nikah mawquf): A marriage contract whose effectiveness is suspended upon the approval of the master.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract to purchase his freedom by paying installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a male slave marries without his master's permission, the marriage is:
A) Batil (void) and cannot be ratified.
B) Valid but its effect is suspended upon the master's permission.
C) Valid and effective from the start.
D) Fasid (irregular) and requires a new contract.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position is that a slave's marriage without permission is valid but suspended, relying on the hadith and the distinction between marriage as a defect versus divorce as its removal.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
12 min
Slave's Marriage Without Master's Permission: Validity vs Enforceability
Debates validity and enforceability of slave's marriage without master's permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school regards a male slave's marriage without master's permission as valid but non-binding (ṣaḥīḥ ghayr nāfidh), and contrast this with the Maliki position using the respective proofs.

Core Idea
A slave's marriage without master's permission is valid but not binding — a key application of the Hanafi distinction between ṣiḥḥa and nafādh.

This lesson examines a classic inter-madhhab dispute: what is the legal status of a slave who marries without his master's permission? While the Hanafi school holds that such a marriage is valid but non-binding (suspended on the master's consent), Imam Malik argues it is fully binding. Understanding this difference requires appreciating two key points: the Hanafi distinction between validity and enforceability, and the role of analogy (qiyās) in legal reasoning. We will trace the arguments, proofs, and the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf) of this disagreement.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on male slave's marriage
Identify the precise ruling: the marriage is valid (ṣaḥīḥ) but non-binding (ghayr nāfidh). It becomes binding only if the master permits. Note the difference for a female slave: her marriage is invalid without consent.
Hanafis distinguish between a male and female slave because the female's virtue belongs to the master.
2
Identify Imam Malik's opposing view
Imam Malik holds that a male slave's marriage is fully valid and binding even without the master's permission. He does not distinguish between male and female in this regard.
The dispute is specific to male slaves; for female slaves, Hanafis and Malikis agree that the marriage is invalid without consent.
3
Follow Malik's proof (qiyās)
Malik argues that since the slave owns divorce (he can divorce his wife without permission), he must also own marriage because divorce is the removal of marriage, and ownership of removal implies ownership of creation.
This is an analogy (qiyās) from the act of divorce to the act of marriage.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes between removing a defect (divorce) and creating a defect (marriage). Marriage is considered a defect for the slave because it restricts the master's use. The slave cannot create a defect in himself regarding the master's property. Additionally, the Hanafis cite the hadith of Jabir: 'Any slave who marries without his master's permission is a fornicator.'
The hadith is a direct textual proof (dalīl naqlī) that the marriage is not proper without permission.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilāf (practical consequence)
Under Hanafi law, the marriage is in limbo until the master consents; if he refuses, it is void. Under Maliki law, the marriage is immediately binding and the master cannot dissolve it. This difference affects the rights of the slave, the master, and any spouse.
The dispute is not merely academic; it changes the actual legal outcome.

اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد چہارم
۱۵۱
كتاب النكاح
اسلئے کہ غلام طلاق کا مالک ہے لہذا نکاح کا بھی مالک ہوگا اور ہماری دلیل حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم کا قول ہے جس غلام نے بغیر اپنے مولی کی اجازت کے نکاح کیا وہ زانی ہے۔ اور اسلئے کہ ان دونوں کے نکاح کو نافذ کرنے میں دونوں کو عیب دار بنانا ہے۔ کیونکہ نکاح دونوں میں عیب ہے۔ پس وہ دونوں اس کے مالک نہیں ہوں گے بغیر اپنے مولی کی اجازت کے۔ تشریح لا يجوز کا ایک مطلب تو یہ ہے کہ بغیر اذن مولیٰ کے غلام اور باندی کا نکاح منعقد ہی نہیں ہو گا۔ حافظ الدین اسی کے قائل ہیں۔ لیکن صحیح مطلب یہ ہے کہ بغیر اذن مولی کے غلام اور باندی کا نکاح جائز ہے اور صحیح ہے۔ لیکن غیر نافذ ہے۔ اس کا نفاذ مولی کی اجازت پر موقوف رہے گا۔ اگر مولیٰ نے اجازت دے دی تو نافذ ہوگا۔ اور اگر اجازت نہ دی تو نافذ نہیں ہوگا سعید ابن المسیب ،حسن اور ابراہیم مخفی کا یہی قول ہے۔
حاصل یہ کہ باندی کا نکاح بغیر اذن مولی کے بالا تفاق نافذ نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ باندی کے منافع بضع مولیٰ کی ملک ہیں۔ اور مولی کی ملک میں بغیر اس کی اجازت کے کسی کو تصرف کا حق نہیں ہے لہذا بغیر مولی کی اجازت کے باندی کا نکاح درست ( نافذ ) نہیں ہوگا۔ اور ربا غلام سواس میں امام مالک کا اختلاف ہے وہ فرماتے ہیں کہ بغیر اذن مولیٰ کے غلام کا نکاح جائز ( نافذ ہے۔ اور احناف کے نزدیک غلام کا نکاح بغیر اذن مولی کے جائز (نافذ ) نہیں ہے۔ امام مالک کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ غلام طلاق کا مالک ہے۔ اور جو شخص طلاق کا مالک ہے وہ نکاح کا بھی مالک ہوگا اسلئے کہ طلاق سبب نکاح کی وجہ سے ہے اور جو شخص کسی چیز کا مالک ہو گا وہ اسکے سبب موصل الیہ کا بھی مالک ہو گا۔ لہذا غلام جب طلاق کا مالک ہے تو اس کے سبب ( نکاح ) کا بھی مال ہو گا۔ اس دلیل کو اس طرح بھی کہا جا سکتا ہے کہ غلام طلاق کا مالک ہے اور طلاق کہتے ہیں رفع نکاح کو اور جو شخص رفع شی کا مالک ہے وہی وضع شی کا بھی مالک ہوگا۔ لہذا غلام نکاح کا بھی مالک ہوگا۔ احناف کی دلیل حدیث جابر ہے۔ جس غلام نے بغیر اذن مولی کے نکاح کیا وہ زانی ہے۔ اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ بغیر اجازت مولی کے نکاح درست ( نافذ ) نہیں ہے۔ دلیل عقلی کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ غلام اور باندی کے نکاح کو نافذ کرنے میں ان کو عیب دار بنانا ہے۔ کیونکہ غلام اور باندی میں نکاح عیب ہے ( یہی وجہ ہے کہ اگر کسی شخص نے غلام یا باندی کو خریدا۔ پھر معلوم ہوا کہ وہ شادی شدہ ہے تو مشتری کے لئے خیار عیب کی وجہ سے ان کو واپس کرنے کا حق ہے ) اور غلام اور باندی کے لئے یہ اختیار نہیں ہے کہ وہ اپنے اندر عیب پیدا کرے حق موٹی کی رعایت کرتے ہوئے پس وہ دونوں بغیر اذن مولی کے نکاح کے مالک نہیں ہوں گے۔ امام مالک کی دلیل کا جواب یہ ہے کہ طلاق ازالہ عیب ہے اور نکاح عیب دار بنانا ہے۔ اور ازالہ عیب کے مالک ہونے سے یہ لازم نہیں آتا کہ وہ شخص تعیب (عیب دار بنانا ) کا بھی مالک ہو۔
Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu Sharh of Hidayah Volume 4, Page 151. Book of Nikah. Because the slave owns divorce, so he would also own marriage. And our proof is the statement of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Any slave who marries without his master's permission is a fornicator.' And because making their marriage effective would be making them defective, since marriage is a defect in both. So they are not owners of it without their master's permission. Commentary: One meaning of 'la yajuz' is that the marriage of a male slave and a female slave without the master's permission is not contracted at all. Hafiz al-Din holds this. But the correct meaning is that the marriage of a male slave and a female slave without the master's permission is permissible and valid, but non-binding. Its enforceability is suspended upon the master's permission. If the master gives permission, it becomes binding; if not, it does not. This is the saying of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib, Hasan, and Ibrahim al-Nakha'i. The conclusion is that the marriage of a female slave without the master's permission is unanimously not binding, because the usufruct of the slave-girl's [vagina] belongs to the master, and no one has the right to dispose of the master's property without his permission. Therefore, without the master's permission, the marriage of a slave-girl is not valid (binding). As for the male slave, there is a difference of opinion with Imam Malik. He says that a male slave's marriage without permission is valid (binding). According to the Hanafis, a male slave's marriage without permission is not valid (binding). Imam Malik's proof is that the slave owns divorce, and whoever owns divorce also owns marriage because divorce is a consequence of marriage; whoever owns a thing also owns its means. Thus, since the slave owns divorce, he owns its cause (marriage). This proof can also be stated as: the slave owns divorce, and divorce means the removal of marriage; whoever owns the removal of a thing owns its establishment. So the slave would own marriage. The Hanafis' proof is the hadith of Jabir: 'Any slave who marries without his master's permission is a fornicator.' This hadith indicates that without the master's permission, the marriage is not valid (binding). The rational proof is that making the slave's marriage binding makes them defective, because marriage is a defect in slaves (this is why if someone buys a slave and then discovers they are married, the buyer has the option of return due to defect). And the slave does not have the authority to create a defect in themselves while observing the master's right. So they are not owners of marriage without the master's permission. The response to Imam Malik's proof is that divorce removes a defect, while marriage creates a defect; ownership of removing a defect does not necessitate ownership of creating a defect.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam Malik regarding the validity and enforceability of a slave's marriage without the master's permission. The Hanafi position, which is the relied-upon view, distinguishes between a male slave and a female slave. The marriage of a female slave without the master's permission is unanimously unenforceable because her virtue belongs to her master. For a male slave, the Hanafis hold that his marriage without permission is valid (sahih) but not binding (ghayr nafidh); it is suspended upon the master's consent. This is based on the hadith of Jabir reported by the Prophet (peace be upon him) declaring such a marriage as zina (fornication), indicating it is not proper. In contrast, Imam Malik argues that a male slave owns divorce, and since he can dissolve marriage, he must also be able to contract it. The Hanafi response is that divorce removes a defect, while marriage creates a defect (for the slave), so the analogy does not hold. The text also notes that Hafiz al-Din held the stricter view that the marriage is not contracted at all, but the correct view (as per al-Hidayah) is that it is valid but suspended.

Key Terms
نفاذ (nafadh): Enforceability or binding effect of a contract; in this context, whether the marriage takes legal effect without the master's permission.
خیار عیب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option to rescind a sale due to a defect in the item; here applied to buying a slave who is married, as marriage is considered a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the consequence for a male slave who marries without his master's permission?
A) The marriage is completely invalid (bāṭil).
B) The marriage is valid and immediately binding.
C) The marriage is valid but non-binding until the master consents.
D) The marriage is valid only if the slave is a mukātib.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school maintains that a male slave's marriage without his master's permission is valid in form but non-binding in effect, a key example of the distinction between validity (ṣiḥḥa) and enforceability (nafādh).

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Marriage of Mukatib, Mudabbar, and Umm Walad
Extends the ruling of slave marriage to other partially free individuals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for the marriage of a mukatib, mudabbar, and umm walad, and explain why the same principle of slave marriage applies despite their partial freedom.

Core Idea
Mukatib, mudabbar, and umm walad remain slaves in marriage because marriage is not earning—only earning restriction is lifted.

In the previous lesson, we learned that a full slave's marriage requires the master's permission and is valid but non-binding without it. Now, the Hidayah extends this ruling to three categories of partially free individuals: the mukatib (slave buying his freedom), the mudabbar (slave freed upon master's death), and the umm walad (slave who bore her master's child). The key insight is that these individuals have been granted some freedoms in earning, but not in marriage, because marriage entails financial liabilities that are not considered profitable.

Guided Path
1
Recall the base ruling for a full slave
A full slave's marriage requires the master's permission. Without it, the marriage is valid but non-binding (sahih ghayr nafidh).
This is the starting point for the analogy.
2
Identify the new categories
The text extends the ruling to three categories: mukatib (slave under kitabah), mudabbar (slave freed after master's death), and umm walad (slave mother of master's child).
Each has some rights of freedom but not full liberty.
3
Understand the illa for the base ruling
The illa (effective cause) is that a slave is restricted from all transactions, and marriage is not a means of earning (kash); rather it imposes liabilities.
Marriage is not considered profitable like trade or work.
4
Apply the same illa to the new categories
Kitabah only lifts the restriction in matters of earning, not in marriage. Hence, like a full slave, these individuals need the master's permission for their own marriage.
The illa remains valid because ownership still exists.
5
Note the exception: marrying off a female slave
A mukatib can marry off his female slave without permission because that brings mahr and nafaqah, which are earnings.
This confirms the rule that earning-related transactions are permitted.

Source Text

الملك فيهما قائم

میں موجود ہے۔
تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ مکاتب کا نکاح بھی بغیر مولی کی اجازت کے صحی ( نافذ نہیں ہے۔ حاصل دلیل یہ ہے کہ غلام ہر قسم کے تصرف سے مجبور ہوتا ہے عقد کتابت نے اسکے حجر ( ممانعت ) کو کسب یعنی منافع حاصل کرنے کے حق میں باطل کر دیا تا کہ کب کے ذریعہ شرافت حریت کو حاصل کر سکے۔ اور نکاح ایسا تصرف نہیں ہے۔ جس میں تحصیل منافع ہو بلکہ اس سے ایک گونہ ضر رثابت ہوگا۔ مثلاً مہر لازم ہوگا۔ اور نفقہ واجب ہوگا۔ لہذا مکاتب نکاح کے حق میں جس طرح پہلے رقیق تھا۔ اسی طرح مکاتب ہونے کے بعد بھی رقیق رہے گا۔ اور چونکہ مکاتب نکاح کے حق میں رقیق ہے۔ اسی وجہ سے وہ اپنے غلام کے نکاح کرنے کا مجاز نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ غلام کا نکاح کمائی کے باب سے نہیں ہے۔ البتہ مکاتب اپنی باندی کا نکاح کر سکتا ہے۔ کیونکہ باندی کا نکاح کمائی کا ذریعہ ہے بایں طور کہ اس کی وجہ سے مہر اور نفقہ حاصل ہوگا۔ اسلئے کہ جو مہر عقد یا دخول کی وجہ سے باندی کیلئے واجب ہوا ہے وہ مولیٰ کیلئے ہو گا۔ یہی حکم مکاتبہ باندی کا ہے کہ اپنی باندی کا نکاح کر سکتی ہے۔ لیکن اپنا نکاح بغیر مولی کی اجازت کے نہیں کر سکتی ہے۔ سابقہ دلیل کی وجہ سے اسی طرح مد براور ام ولد بغیر مولی کی اجازت کے نکاح کے مجاز نہیں ہیں۔ اسلئے کہ ملک دونوں میں موجود ہے۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ اگر مولی نے کل مملوک لی حر کہا تو مد براورام ولد دونوں آزاد ہو جائیں گے۔
The marriage of a mukatib is also valid only with the master's permission. And likewise the mukatib, because the contract of kitabah necessitated the removal of restriction regarding earning, so he remains under the ruling of slavery regarding marriage. For this reason, the mukatib does not own the right to marry off his male slave, but he owns the right to marry off his female slave, because that falls under the category of earning. Similarly, the mukatibah (female mukatib) does not own the right to marry herself without the master's permission, but she owns the right to marry off her female slave, for the same reason mentioned. And likewise the mudabbar and umm walad, because ownership (milk) exists in both. Translation: The same ruling applies to the mukatib. Because the contract of kitabah necessitated the removal of restriction regarding earning, so he remains under the ruling of slavery regarding marriage. For this reason, the mukatib does not own the right to marry off his male slave, but he owns the right to marry off his female slave, because that falls under the category of earning. Similarly, the mukatibah does not own the right to marry herself without the master's permission, but she owns the right to marry off her female slave, for the same reason. And the same ruling applies to the mudabbar and umm walad, because ownership exists in both. Commentary: The issue is that the marriage of a mukatib without the master's permission is valid but not binding. The gist of the proof is that a slave is restricted from all transactions. The contract of kitabah nullified this restriction only in terms of earning (to enable him to acquire wealth to pay for his freedom), but not in terms of marriage, because marriage is not a means of earning; rather it entails liabilities such as mahr and nafaqah. Therefore, the mukatib remains like a slave regarding marriage. Hence, he cannot marry off his male slave (since that is not earning), but he can marry off his female slave because that brings mahr and nafaqah, which are earnings. The same applies to the mukatibah. Similarly, the mudabbar and umm walad are not permitted to marry without the master's permission because ownership (of the master) over them still exists. This is why if the master says 'all my property is free,' both the mudabbar and umm walad become free.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage expands the previous ruling on slave marriage to other categories of partially free individuals: the mukatib (a slave who has contracted to buy his freedom), the mudabbar (a slave whose freedom is conditional upon the master's death), and the umm walad (a slave-girl who has borne a child to her master). The key principle is that these individuals, despite having some rights of freedom, remain slaves regarding marriage because marriage is not considered a profit-earning activity (except when marrying off a female slave, which brings mahr). The contract of kitabah only lifts the restriction on earning (kash) to allow the slave to work and pay for freedom, but does not remove the restriction on marriage. Therefore, like a full slave, they need their master's permission to marry themselves. However, a mukatib or mukatibah can marry off their own female slaves (if they own any) because that generates income (mahr and nafaqah). The same logic applies to mudabbar and umm walad due to the continued ownership of the master over them.

Key Terms
مکاتب (mukatib): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah (written agreement) to earn and pay a specified sum to his master in exchange for freedom.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose manumission is deferred until after his master's death (via tadbir).
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who bears a child to her master; she becomes free upon his death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can a mukatib marry off his own female slave without the master's permission?
A) Because he owns her completely.
B) Because it is a form of earning (kash) through mahr and nafaqah.
C) Because the master's permission is only needed for his own marriage.
D) Because a female slave is not considered property.

Key Takeaway
Mukatib, mudabbar, and umm walad are like full slaves in marriage because only the restriction on earning is lifted by kitabah; marriage is not earning.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
6 min
Payment of Mahr When a Slave Marries with Permission
Ruling on how mahr is collected from a slave, and exception for mudabbar and mukatib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on how mahr is collected from a slave and distinguish between a regular slave and a mudabbar/mukatib regarding payment method.

Core Idea
The slave's mahr is a debt on his person; he can be sold to pay it, but a mudabbar or mukatib must pay from earnings as their potential freedom prevents sale.

In Hanafi law, a slave's mahr is a debt that attaches to his person (raqabah). This lesson explains the mechanism of collection: the slave can be sold to satisfy the debt, similar to trade debts. However, a mudabbar or mukatib—who have a right to eventual freedom—cannot be sold; they must work and pay from their earnings. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the correct ruling in cases of slave marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for a regular slave
The passage states that when a slave marries with his master's permission, the mahr becomes a debt on his person (raqabah). He can be sold to pay it.
The master's permission is a condition for the debt to attach to the slave's person.
2
Identify the exception for mudabbar and mukatib
For a mudabbar (slave promised freedom after master's death) and a mukatib (slave under contract of freedom), they cannot be sold for mahr. Instead, they must work (sa'y) and pay from their earnings.
The exception is due to their protected interest in eventual freedom.
3
Understand the proof via analogy (qiyas) to trade debts
The ruling is analogized to a debt from trade (dayn al-tijarah), where a slave can be sold to pay trade debts. The common illa is that both debts arise from the slave's actions with the master's consent and creditors need satisfaction.
The analogy is explicit in the text: 'kama fi dayn al-tijarah'.
4
Analyze the limit of the analogy for mudabbar and mukatib
Mudabbar and mukatib are not subject to transfer from one ownership to another because tadbir and kitabah confer a right to freedom. Selling them would void that right, so the debt must be paid from earnings instead.
The inability to be sold is due to their special status, not the nature of the debt.
5
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Given a specific case (e.g., a regular slave vs. a mudabbar), determine the method of mahr collection based on the slave's status.
Always check if the slave is a mudabbar or mukatib first.

Source Text

غلام نے آقا کی اجازت سے نکاح کیا تو مہر کے بدلے غلام کو بیچا جائے گا
واذا تزوج العبد باذن مولاه فالمهردين فى رقبته يباع فيه لان هذادين وجب فى رقبة العبد لوجود سببه من اهله وقد ظهر فى حق المولى لصدور الاذن من جهته فيتعلق برقبته دفعا للمضرة عن اصحاب الديون کمافی دين التجارة والمدير والمكاتب يسعيان في المهر ولايبا عان فيه لانهما لا يحتملان النقل من ملك الى ملك مع بقاء الكتابة والتدبير فيودى من كسبهما لا من نفسهما
ترجمہ اور جب نکاح کیا غلام نے اپنے مولی کی اجازت سے تو مہر غلام کے رقبہ میں دین ہوگا۔ اس دین میں غلام کو فروخت کیا جائے گا۔ اسلئے کہ یہ دین غلام کے رقبہ میں واجب ہوا سبب دین کے پائے جانے کی وجہ سے اس کے اہل سے اور دین ظاہر ہو گا مولی کے حق میں مولیٰ کی جانب سے اجازت کے صادر ہونے کی وجہ سے۔ پس دین متعلق ہو گا رقبہ عبد کیسا تھ اصحاب دیون سے مضرت کو دفع کرنے کیلئے جیسا کہ دین تجارت میں اور مدبر اور مکاتب مہر ( کی ادائیگی ) میں سعی کریں گے ان دونوں کو مہر میں فروخت نہیں کیا جائے گا کیونکہ وہ دونوں ایک ملک سے دوسرے ملک کی طرف منتقل ہونے کا احتمال نہیں رکھتے ہیں تدبیر اور کتابت کے باقی رہتے ہوئے۔ پس مہران دونوں کی کمائی سے ادا کیا جائے گا نہ کہ ان دونوں کی ذات سے۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر غلام نے مولی کی اجازت سے نکاح کیا تو مہر غلام پر واجب ہوگا اور اس مہر کو ادا کرنے کیلئے غلام
If a slave marries with his master's permission, then the mahr is a debt on his person (raqabah); he will be sold to pay it. Because this debt became obligatory on the slave's person due to the existence of its cause from those qualified, and it becomes apparent in the master's right due to his granting permission. So it attaches to his person to prevent harm to the creditors, just like a debt of trade. As for the mudabbar and mukatib, they will strive (sai) to pay the mahr, and they will not be sold for it, because they are not subject to transfer from one ownership to another while the tadbir and kitabah remain. So it will be paid from their earnings, not from their persons. Translation: And when a slave marries with his master's permission, the mahr becomes a debt on his person. He will be sold for this debt. Because this debt became obligatory on the slave's person due to the existence of its cause from those qualified, and it becomes apparent in the master's right due to his granting permission. So it attaches to his person to prevent harm to the creditors, just like a debt of trade. As for the mudabbar and mukatib, they will strive (sai) to pay the mahr, and they will not be sold for it, because they are not subject to transfer from one ownership to another while the tadbir and kitabah remain. So it will be paid from their earnings, not from their persons. Commentary: The issue is that if a slave marries with his master's permission, the mahr is owed by the slave, and to collect it, the slave can be sold.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage discusses the mechanism of paying mahr when a slave marries with permission. The mahr becomes a debt (dayn) on the slave's person (raqabah), meaning it attaches to his physical being as a slave. Since the master permitted the marriage, the debt is valid against the slave, and the creditor (the wife) can claim it. If the slave cannot pay, he can be sold to satisfy the debt, similar to debts incurred in trade (dayn al-tijarah). However, for the mudabbar and mukatib, who have a right to eventual freedom via tadbir or kitabah, they cannot be sold because that would negate their status. Instead, they must work (sa'y) to pay the mahr from their earnings. The key difference is that a full slave is a commodity that can be transferred, while a mudabbar and mukatib have a protected interest that prevents their sale. The mahr for them is paid from their acquisition (kash), not their person.

Key Terms
رقبہ (raqabah): The slave's person or physical being as the subject of ownership; debts can attach to the raqabah.
سعی (sa'y): The effort or work a mukatib or mudabbar does to earn money to pay obligations like mahr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the difference between a regular slave and a mudabbar regarding payment of mahr?
A) A regular slave cannot be sold for mahr, while a mudabbar can.
B) A regular slave is sold to pay mahr, while a mudabbar pays from his earnings.
C) Both are sold to pay mahr.
D) The master pays the mahr for both.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a slave's mahr attaches to his person and he can be sold to pay it, but a mudabbar or mukatib must pay from their earnings because their potential freedom prevents sale.

Marriage of a Mukatab without Permission
Ruling on marriage of a mukatab without permission, with talil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a mukatab's marriage without his master's permission and explain the talil (legal reasoning) that distinguishes marriage from earning.

Core Idea
Kitabah lifts restrictions only for earning; marriage requires permission because it imposes financial harm.

In this passage, the author explains the legal capacity of a mukatab (a slave under a kitabah contract) regarding marriage. The key principle drawn from the contract is that it only allows the mukatab to engage in earning activities to buy his freedom. Marriage, however, is not considered an earning activity—it imposes obligations like mahr and nafaqah that harm the master's interests. Therefore, the mukatab's marriage without the master's permission is not valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the Hanafi ruling on the marriage of a mukatab without his master's permission: it is not valid (ghayr nafidh).
2
Understand the talil (legal reasoning)
The talil centers on the distinction between earning (kasb) and marriage. Kitabah only removes the restriction (hajr) for earning, not for marriage. Marriage is considered a harmful transaction (mudirr) because it imposes mahr and nafaqah, which are not beneficial to the master.
The passage uses the phrase 'ضرر ثابت' (harm is established) to emphasize this point.
3
Apply the principle to related cases
Use the same talil to derive rulings for: (a) a mukatab marrying his male slave vs. female slave, (b) a female mukatab marrying herself vs. her female slave, (c) mudabbar and umm walad.
Notice that the mukatab can marry his female slave (bandi) because it brings mahr and nafaqah, which are earnings; he cannot marry his male slave (ghulam) because that does not yield profit.

Source Text

تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ مکاتب کا نکاح بھی بغیر مولی کی اجازت کے صحی ( نافذ نہیں ہے۔ حاصل دلیل یہ ہے کہ غلام ہر قسم کے تصرف سے مجبور ہوتا ہے عقد کتابت نے اسکے حجر ( ممانعت ) کو کسب یعنی منافع حاصل کرنے کے حق میں باطل کر دیا تا کہ کب کے ذریعہ شرافت حریت کو حاصل کر سکے۔ اور نکاح ایسا تصرف نہیں ہے۔ جس میں تحصیل منافع ہو بلکہ اس سے ایک گونہ ضر رثابت ہوگا۔ مثلاً مہر لازم ہوگا۔ اور نفقہ واجب ہوگا۔ لہذا مکاتب نکاح کے حق میں جس طرح پہلے رقیق تھا۔ اسی طرح مکاتب ہونے کے بعد بھی رقیق رہے گا۔ اور چونکہ مکاتب نکاح کے حق میں رقیق ہے۔ اسی وجہ سے وہ اپنے غلام کے نکاح کرنے کا مجاز نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ غلام کا نکاح کمائی کے باب سے نہیں ہے۔ البتہ مکاتب اپنی باندی کا نکاح کر سکتا ہے۔ کیونکہ باندی کا نکاح کمائی کا ذریعہ ہے بایں طور کہ اس کی وجہ سے مہر اور نفقہ حاصل ہوگا۔ اسلئے کہ جو مہر عقد یا دخول کی وجہ سے باندی کیلئے واجب ہوا ہے وہ مولیٰ کیلئے ہو گا۔ یہی حکم مکاتبہ باندی کا ہے کہ اپنی باندی کا نکاح کر سکتی ہے۔ لیکن اپنا نکاح بغیر مولی کی اجازت کے نہیں کر سکتی ہے۔ سابقہ دلیل کی وجہ سے اسی طرح مد براورام ولد بغیر مولی کی اجازت کے نکاح کے مجاز نہیں ہیں۔ اسلئے کہ ملک دونوں میں موجود ہے۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ اگر مولی نے کل مملوک لی حر کہا تو مد براورام ولد دونوں آزاد ہو جائیں گے ۔
Translation: And this same ruling applies to the mukatab. Because the contract of kitabah has made it obligatory to remove the prohibition in the matter of earning. So with respect to marriage, he will remain upon the ruling of slavery. And for this reason, the mukatab does not own the marriage of his male slave, but he owns the marriage of his female slave. Because this is from the chapter of earning. Similarly, a female mukatab does not own the right to contract her own marriage without the master's permission, but she owns the marriage of her female slave. Due to the same proof we have mentioned. And the same ruling applies to the mudabbar and umm walad, because ownership exists in both of them. Explanation: The issue is that the marriage of a mukatab without the master's permission is not valid (not effective). The gist of the proof is that a slave is prevented from all types of transactions. The contract of kitabah annulled his prohibition (hajr) with respect to earning, i.e., acquiring profit, so that he may obtain the honor of freedom through earning. But marriage is not a transaction in which profit is acquired; rather, some harm is established from it, e.g., mahr becomes obligatory, and nafaqah becomes obligatory. Therefore, the mukatab, with respect to marriage, remains a slave just as he was before being a mukatab. And since the mukatab is a slave with respect to marriage, for this reason he is not permitted to perform the marriage of his male slave, because the marriage of a male slave is not from the chapter of earning. However, the mukatab may perform the marriage of his female slave, because the marriage of a female slave is a means of earning, in that through it mahr and nafaqah are obtained. Because whatever mahr becomes obligatory for the female slave due to contract or consummation, it will belong to the master. The same ruling applies to the female mukatab: she may perform the marriage of her female slave, but she may not perform her own marriage without the master's permission, due to the same proof. Similarly, the mudabbar and umm walad are not permitted to marry without the master's permission, because ownership exists in both of them. For this reason, if the master says to all his mamluks, 'You are free,' then the mudabbar and umm walad will both become free.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement explaining the legal capacity of a mukatab (a slave who has contracted with his master to earn his freedom) regarding marriage. The core principle is that the contract of kitabah only lifts the restriction (hajr) on the slave's ability to earn and acquire wealth, so he can pay the instalments of his freedom price. However, marriage is not considered an act of earning; it imposes financial obligations like mahr and nafaqah, which are detrimental from the master's perspective. Therefore, in matters of marriage, the mukatab remains in the same state of slavery (riqq) as before. Consequently, his marriage without the master's permission is not valid (ghayr nafidh). He cannot marry his male slave (ghulam) because that does not bring profit, but he can marry his female slave (bandi) because it generates mahr for him, which becomes the master's property. The same ruling applies to a female mukatab: she can marry her female slave (earning mahr) but cannot marry herself without permission. The mudabbar (slave freed upon master's death) and umm walad (slave mother of master's child) are also subject to the same restriction because full ownership remains vested in the master. The Hanafi position is that marriage without permission is invalid for these categories of slaves due to the master's right of ownership.

Key Terms
مکاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract (kitabah) with his master to earn his freedom by paying a sum of money in instalments.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been declared by the master to be free upon the master's death (tadbir).
ام ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has given birth to a child recognized by her master.
حجر (hajr): Legal restriction or prohibition on a person's capacity to engage in transactions.
کسب (kasb): Earning, acquisition of profit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because a female slave is of lesser status and can be given away freely.
B) Because the marriage of a female slave brings mahr and nafaqah which are earnings, while the marriage of a male slave does not.
C) Because the master's permission is not needed for female slaves.
D) Because the mukatab owns his female slave but not his male slave.

Key Takeaway
The marriage of a mukatab without his master's permission is invalid because kitabah only lifts the restriction for earning, not for marriage, which imposes financial harm.

Nikah — Marriage

Marriage of a Slave with Permission: Mahr as a Debt on His Person
Ruling on mahr being a debt on the slave's person when married with permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that mahr becomes a debt on the slave's person when he marries with permission, and distinguish the exception for mudabbar/mukatab slaves.

Core Idea
Mahr of a slave with permission is a debt on his person, leading to sale, except mudabbar/mukatab who pay from earnings.

In this lesson, we examine a specific ruling in Hanafi fiqh regarding the marriage of a slave who has obtained his master's permission. The key question is how the mahr is to be paid. The Hanafi school holds that it becomes a debt on the slave's person (raqabah), which can be enforced by selling the slave. However, an exception exists for slaves who are mudabbar (promised freedom upon master's death) or mukatab (contracted to purchase freedom). These categories cannot be sold because their special status would be nullified. Instead, they must earn and pay from their income. We will explore the reasoning behind these rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states that when a slave marries with permission, the mahr is a debt on his person, and he is sold to pay it.
2
Understand the cause and removal of impediment
Marriage is the cause (sabab) that obligates mahr. The master's right (impeding the debt on the slave) is removed by his permission. Hence the debt attaches to the slave's person.
The underlying principle: 'al-sabab mawjud wa al-mani' muntafi'
3
Apply the analogy with trade debt
Just as a ma'dhun slave (authorized to trade) is sold for trade debts, an ordinary slave is sold for the mahr debt. This analogy (qiyas) is used by the Hanafis.
Analogy based on the common illa: debt incurred with permission to repel harm from creditors.
4
Note the exception for mudabbar/mukatab
Mudabbar and mukatab slaves are not sold because their ownership transfer (sale) would conflict with the ongoing tadbir/kitabah. They must pay from their earnings.
The inability to transfer ownership (naql min milk ila milk) with tadbir/kitabah intact prevents sale.
5
Consider the practical limitation
If the sale price does not cover the full mahr, the slave is sold only once; the remainder becomes a demand after freedom.
This is to avoid excessive harm (daf' al-darar) as per Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text

ان کو فروخت بھی نہیں کیا جا سکتا ہے۔ حاصل یہ کہ مدبر اور مکاتب کے رقبہ سے مہر وصول کرنا تعد ر ہے۔ لہذا یہ دونوں کما کر مہر ادا کریں گے۔
Translation: And when a slave marries with his master's permission, the mahr is a debt upon his person (raqabah). He will be sold for it. Because this is a debt that became obligatory on the slave's person due to the existence of its cause from its people (i.e., the slave himself), and it becomes apparent in the master's right because permission issued from him. So it attaches to his person to repel harm from the creditors, just as in the case of trade debt. As for the mudabbar and mukatab, they work to pay the mahr and are not sold for it, because they are not capable of being transferred from one ownership to another while the tadbir and kitabah remain. So it is paid from their earnings, not from their persons. Explanation: The scenario is that if a slave marries with his master's permission, the mahr is obligatory on the slave, and to pay that mahr, the slave will be sold. However, if the entire mahr cannot be paid from his sale price, he will not be sold again; rather, the remaining mahr will be demanded after he becomes free. The gist of the proof is that the debt of mahr became wajib on the slave's person, and every debt that becomes wajib on the slave's person results in the slave being sold for that debt. Therefore, this slave will also be sold to pay the mahr debt. The proof of the obligation itself is that the cause of obligation, i.e., marriage, came from someone who is capable (sane, adult), and the impediment, i.e., the master's right, was removed by his permission. The reason for the obligation on the slave's person is the master's permission and to repel harm from the creditors, i.e., women. Just as a ma'dhun slave (authorized for trade) is sold for trade debts, similarly the slave will be sold to pay the wife's mahr. However, if a mudabbar or mukatab marries with the master's permission, they will work to pay the mahr and will not be sold. The proof is that transferring a mudabbar or mukatab from one ownership to another while tadbir and kitabah remain is not valid. Therefore, when they are not capable of being transferred in ownership, they cannot be sold. The conclusion is that recovering the mahr from their persons is oppression (tahdi), so they will pay it from their earnings.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling when a slave marries with his master's permission, and how the mahr (dower) is to be paid. The Hanafi ruling is that the mahr becomes a debt (dayn) upon the slave's person (raqabah), meaning the slave himself can be sold to satisfy this debt. The reasoning is that marriage is a valid cause of obligation (sabab), and the master's permission removes the impediment (mani') of the master's right. Thus, the debt attaches to the slave's person just like a trade debt for a ma'dhun slave (a slave authorized to trade). However, this rule is specific to an ordinary slave (raqeeq). For a mudabbar (slave with tadbir) or a mukatab (slave under kitabah), they cannot be sold because their status (tadbir or kitabah) would be nullified by transfer of ownership. Therefore, they must work and pay the mahr from their earnings (kasb). The commentary further clarifies that if the slave is sold but the sale price does not cover the full mahr, he is sold only once; the remaining debt is deferred until he becomes free (upon the master's death or completion of kitabah), after which it can be claimed. This is a practical concession to avoid excessive harm.

Key Terms
رقبہ (raqabah): The physical person of the slave, considered as a subject of ownership and liability.
عبد مأذون (abd ma'dhun): A slave who has been given permission by his master to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the difference between an ordinary slave and a mudabbar/mukatab regarding payment of mahr when they marry with permission?
A) The ordinary slave is sold to pay the mahr, while the mudabbar/mukatab pay from their earnings.
B) The ordinary slave pays from his earnings, while the mudabbar/mukatab are sold.
C) Both are sold, but the mudabbar/mukatab are sold only once.
D) There is no difference; both are sold.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the mahr of a slave who marries with permission becomes a debt on his person (raqabah), enforceable by sale—except for mudabbar/mukatab, who pay from earnings.

Master's Words of Divorce Not Permission for Marriage
Ruling on whether master's command to divorce constitutes permission for marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the master's command to divorce or separate does not constitute permission for a slave's unauthorized marriage, and identify when such a command does constitute permission.

Core Idea
The master's ambiguous command to divorce is rejection, not permission; only revocable divorce indicates permission.

This lesson addresses a subtle issue in Hanafi marriage law: when a slave marries without permission, and the master later says 'divorce her' or 'separate from her,' does that mean the master approves the marriage? The Hanafi ruling is that such words are not permission—they are rejection. Understanding the reasoning helps us see how Hanafi scholars prioritize context and linguistic usage in legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal effect
The act is the master's utterance. The ruling is whether it constitutes permission for the slave's marriage.
Focus on the legal consequence of the master's words.
2
Recognize the ambiguity
The words 'tallaqha' and 'fariqha' can mean either permission (since divorce assumes a valid marriage) or rejection (since they can also mean ending an invalid union).
Both meanings are linguistically possible.
3
Apply Hanafi method of interpretation
Given the slave's rebelliousness, it is more appropriate to interpret the words as rejection. Additionally, these terms are commonly used for ending invalid contracts.
Context and the slave's disobedience tip the scale.
4
Distinguish the exception
If the master says 'give her a revocable divorce' (tallaqha tatliqatan tamlik al-ruj'ah), this is definitive permission because revocable divorce only applies to a valid marriage.
This removes all ambiguity.
5
Understand the practical consequence
Without permission, the marriage remains invalid and must be terminated. With permission, it is retroactively validated.
The difference affects the status of the marriage.

Because rejecting this contract and abandoning it is called divorce and separation, and that is more appropriate to the rebellious slave's state, or it is more primary; so it is more appropriate to interpret it as such. But if the master said, 'Give her a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i),' then this is permission, because revocable divorce only occurs in a valid marriage, so permission is determined. Explanation: The scenario is that a slave married without his master's permission. When the master learned of it, he said to the slave, 'Divorce her' or 'Separate from her.' This will not be considered permission from the master for the marriage. The proof is that the master's speech carries both the possibility of permission and the possibility of rejection. Because rejecting a fasid (invalid) marriage and abandoning it is called divorce and separation. So when both possibilities exist, we give preference to rejection over permission for two reasons: first, because the slave is rebellious and disobedient, and for a rebellious person, it is appropriate to reject and nullify his action rather than enforce it; second, because... [text cut off]
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage explains how to interpret a master's command when he discovers that his slave has married without permission. If the master says 'Divorce her' (tallaqha) or 'Separate from her' (fariqha), these words are ambiguous. They could be interpreted as permission to continue the marriage (because if the marriage is valid, divorce would be needed to end it) or as disapproval (rejection) of the marriage (since the master is ordering the slave to end it). The Hanafi ruling is that these words are considered rejection, not permission. The reasoning is two-fold: (1) The slave is rebellious (mutamarrid) for marrying without permission, so it is more fitting to reject his act rather than validate it. (2) Since the words 'divorce' and 'separation' are also used to terminate an invalid marriage (nikah fasid), and the slave's marriage is invalid without permission, the master likely intends to end the invalid union. However, if the master specifically says 'give her a revocable divorce' (tallaqha tatliqatan tamlik al-ruj'ah), this clearly presumes a valid marriage (since revocable divorce only applies to a valid nikah), so it amounts to permission (ijazah) of the marriage. This distinction is based on the linguistic and legal usage of these terms in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
اجازة (ijazah): Permission given by the master for a slave to perform a legal act (here, marriage).
طلاق رجعي (talaq raj'i): A revocable divorce, allowing the husband to take back his wife during the waiting period without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because divorce is not valid from a slave.
D) Because the marriage was contracted without witnesses.

Key Takeaway
The master's ambiguous command to divorce or separate is not permission for an unauthorized slave marriage; only a command for revocable divorce serves as permission.

Sale of Slave to Pay Mahr When Married with Permission
Ruling on selling a slave to pay mahr debt after marriage with master's permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on selling a slave who married with permission to pay mahr, and distinguish the exception for mudabbar and mukatab.

Core Idea
A slave who marries with permission is sold to pay mahr; mudabbar and mukatab earn the mahr themselves.

This passage addresses a specific scenario: a slave marries with his master's permission but cannot pay the mahr. How is the mahr to be settled? The Hanafi ruling uses analogy to trade debts: just as a ma'dhun slave is sold for his debts, so too a slave is sold for the mahr debt. However, an important exception applies to mudabbar and mukatab slaves, who cannot be sold due to their special status. By the end, you will understand the underlying qiyas and the scope of the exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
The text states that when a slave marries with his master's permission, he is sold to pay the mahr debt. Note that the mahr becomes a debt on the slave's property (رقبہ).
The master's permission removes the preventer (mani') of marriage, so the marriage is valid and mahr is obligatory.
2
Trace the qiyas (analogy)
The author analogizes this case to a ma'dhun slave's trade debts. Just as a ma'dhun slave is sold for trade debts, a slave is sold for the mahr debt.
The illa is that both are debts on the slave's value (رقبہ).
3
Understand the exception for mudabbar and mukatab
Mudabbar (promised freedom upon master's death) and mukatab (under contract of manumission) are not sold. Instead, they must earn and pay the mahr themselves.
The reason is that they cannot be transferred to another owner while their status exists.
4
Examine the proof for the exception
The text argues that transferring a mudabbar or mukatab is invalid because it would contradict the rights of tadbir and kitabah. Therefore, sale is impossible.
The conclusion: collecting mahr from their property is transgression (تعدی).
5
Apply the principle
In practice, any debt that becomes obligatory on the slave's value (رقبہ) leads to the slave being sold, except when the slave's status prevents transfer of ownership.
This reflects the broader Hanafi principle that debts are to be satisfied from the debtor's assets.

Source Text

He will be sold. However, if the full mahr cannot be paid from the price of this slave, he will not be sold again; rather the remaining mahr will be claimed after he becomes free. The gist of the proof is that the debt of mahr has become obligatory on the slave's property, and every debt that becomes obligatory on the slave's property causes the slave to be sold for that debt. Therefore this slave will also be sold to pay the mahr debt. And the proof for the obligation itself is that the cause of obligation, i.e. marriage, originated from one who is eligible (sane, adult). And the preventer of marriage, i.e. the master's right, has been removed by his permission. And the reason for the obligation on the slave's property is the master's permission and to remove harm from women. However, if a mudabbar or mukatab married with the master's permission, they will earn and pay the mahr; they will not be sold. The proof is that it is not valid to transfer a mudabbar or mukatab from one ownership to another while preserving the tadbir or kitabah. So when these two are not capable of transfer of ownership, they cannot be sold. The conclusion is that collecting the mahr from the property of a mudabbar or mukatab is transgression. Therefore these two will earn and pay the mahr.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding a slave who marries with his master's permission. The ruling is that the mahr becomes a debt encumbering the slave's value, and as a result, the slave is sold to satisfy that debt. The text first states this rule, then provides the proof by way of qiyas (analogy) to trade debts: just as a ma'dhun slave is sold for trade debts, so too for the mahr debt. The reason is that the master's permission removes his right of ownership as a preventer, and the necessity of paying mahr arises from the marriage contract. Exception is made for the mudabbar (slave promised freedom upon master's death) and the mukatab (slave who has contracted manumission). They are not sold because they cannot be transferred to another owner while their status remains; instead, they must earn through their own labor to pay the mahr. The proof for this exception is that transfer of ownership is not possible for them due to the right of tadbir or kitabah.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death.
مکاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission with the master, agreeing to pay a sum of money.
رقبہ (raqabah): The slave's value or property; his entire worth as an asset.

A slave who marries with permission is sold for the mahr debt by qiyas to trade debts, but mudabbar and mukatab are exempt because their status prevents transfer of ownership.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Master's Command 'Give Her Talaq' Not Consent to Slave's Marriage
Ruling with proof on ambiguous master command

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that ambiguous commands after an unauthorised act are construed against the rebel, and distinguish when a master's words constitute consent.

Core Idea
Ambiguous master's command after slave's rebellion is rejection, not consent.

When a slave marries without permission, the master's reaction is critical. Does his command to 'give talaq' signal acceptance of the marriage or a demand to end it? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer rooted in the slave's rebellion and the ease of removal versus revocation. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning and a key exception for revocable divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
A slave contracts marriage without the master's permission. Upon learning of it, the master tells the slave 'give her talaq' or 'separate from her'. Determine whether this statement is a declaration of consent (ijazah) to the marriage.
2
Analyse the linguistic ambiguity
The word 'talaq' can have two meanings: literal (divorce of a valid marriage) or metaphorical (dissolution of a defective contract). The master's words are therefore ambiguous between consent (if the marriage is considered valid) and rejection (if the marriage is defective).
The Hanafi principle: words are construed in favour of the speaker's known intent, but here the speaker's intent is unclear.
3
Apply the two reasons for preferring rejection
First, the slave's rebellious nature makes it more appropriate to reject his act than to validate it. Second, rejecting a defective marriage (daf') is easier than revoking a valid one (raf'), and the master's command is more likely aimed at the simpler action.
This reflects the legal maxim: 'al-daf' ashal min al-raf'' (removal is easier than revocation).
4
Address the objection about literal meaning
If someone argues that 'talaq' literally means divorce, and a divorce can only occur in a valid marriage, so the master must be implying consent, the response is that the contextual indication (the slave's rebellion and the defect of the contract) overrides the literal meaning.
Contextual indication (qarinah) can shift meaning from haqiqah to majaz.
5
Distinguish the exception: revocable divorce
If the master says 'give her one revocable divorce' (talaq raj'i), this does constitute consent because revocable divorce presupposes a valid marriage. Thus the master's command cannot be interpreted as rejection.
The difference lies in the impossibility of applying the command to a defective marriage.

Source Text

واذا تزوج العبد بغير اذن مولاه فقال المولى طلقها او فارقها فليس هذا باجازة لانه يحتمل الرد لان رد هذا العقد ومتاركته يسمى طلاقا و مفارقة وهو اليق بحال العبد المتمرد او هوادنى فكان الحمل عليه اولى وان قال طلقها تطليقة تملك الرجعة فهذا اجازة لان الطلاق الرجعي لا يكون الافى نكاح صحيح فتتعين الاجازة
ترجمہ اور جب نکاح کیا غلام نے اپنے مولی کی بغیر اجازت کے۔ پھر مولی نے کہا اس کو تو طلاق دے دے یا اس کو جدا کر دے تو یہ اجازت نہیں ہے اسلئے کہ یہ قول احتمال رکھتا ہے روکا۔ کیونکہ اس عقد کے رد اور متارکت کا نام طلاق اور مفارقت ہے اور وہ سرکش غلام کے حال کے زیادہ مناسب ہے۔ یا وہ اولیٰ ہے پس اس پر محمول کرنا اولیٰ ہوگا اور اگر مولیٰ نے کہا طلقها تطليقة تملك الرجعة (تو طلاق دے دے ایسی کہ تجھے رجوع کرنے کا اختیار ہو ) تو یہ ( قول) اجازت ہے۔ کیونکہ طلاق رجعی نہیں ہوتی مگر نکاح صحیح میں ۔ پس اجازت متعین ہو گئی ۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ غلام نے بغیر مولی کی اجازت کے نکاح کیا۔ معلوم ہونے کے بعد مولی نے غلام سے کہا طلقها یا فارقها تو یہ مولیٰ کی طرف سے نکاح کی اجازت نہیں ہوگی۔ دلیل یہ کہ مولیٰ کے اس کلام میں جہاں اجازت نکاح کا احتمال ہے اسی کے ساتھ نکاح کو رد کرنے کا بھی احتمال ہے۔ کیونکہ نکاح فاسد کو رد کرنے اور ترک کرنے کا نام طلاق اور مفارقت ہے۔ پس جب دونوں چیزوں کا احتمال ہے تو ہم نے جہت رد کو ترجیح دی۔ جہت اجازت پر دو وجہوں سے۔ اول یہ کہ غلام سرکش اور نافرمان ہے۔ اور متمرد اور سرکش کے مناسب یہی ہے کہ اس کے کئے ہوئے عمل کو رد اور باطل کر دیا جائے نہ یہ کہ اس کو نافذ کیا جائے ، دوم یہ کہ رداد نی
یونکہ ردہ فعل ہے۔ اور طلاق ثبوت نکاح کے بعد دفع ہے اور دفع آسان ہے رفع سے لہذا دفع ( رو ) پر محمول کرنا اولی اور انسب ہوگا اور ا کر کوئی اور کال کرے کہ لفظ طلقها ایقاع طلاق میں حقیقت ہے۔ اور متارکت اور رد کے معنی میں مجاز ہے اور حقیقت پر عمل کرنا بھی ممکن ہے تو پھر مجاز کی طرف کیوں رجوع کیا گیا ہے۔ جواب یہ ہوگا کہ حقیقت کو دلالت حال کی وجہ سے چھوڑ دیا جاتا ہے اور یہاں یہی بات ہے۔ اور اگر مولی نے کبا طلقها تطليقة تملک الرجعة تو یہ قول مولی کی طرف سے اجازت ہوگا ۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ موٹی نے غلام کو حکم دیا ہے کہ وہ طلاق رجعی دے کے اور طلاق رجعی نکاح صحیح کے بعد ہوتی ہے۔ پس مولی کا طلاق رجعی کا حکم دینا گویا نکاح صحیح کی اجازت دیتا ہے۔
And when a slave marries without his master's permission, then the master says 'give her talaq' or 'separate from her', this is not consent, because it is likely to be rejection. Indeed, the rejection of this contract and separation is called talaq and parting, and it is more appropriate for the rebellious slave or is better. So interpreting it that way is preferred. And if he says 'give her one revocable divorce', this is consent, because revocable divorce only occurs in a valid marriage. Thus consent is determined. Translation: ... Explanation: The scenario is that a slave married without the master's permission. When the master found out, he said 'give her talaq' or 'separate from her'. This is not consent. The proof is that the master's words, while possibly indicating consent, also possibly indicate rejection of the marriage. Because the name for rejecting and abandoning a defective marriage is talaq and separation. Since both possibilities exist, we give preference to the aspect of rejection over consent for two reasons. First, the slave is rebellious and disobedient, and for the rebellious, it is more appropriate to reject and invalidate his action rather than enforce it. Second, because rejection is an act, while talaq is removal after establishing the marriage, and removal is easier than revocation. Therefore interpreting it as removal is more appropriate. And if someone objects saying the word 'talaq' is literal in applying divorce and metaphorical for separation and rejection, and it is possible to act upon the literal meaning, why resort to metaphor? The answer is that the literal meaning is abandoned due to contextual indication, and that is the case here. And if the master says 'give her one revocable divorce', this statement is consent. The proof is that the master ordered the slave to give a revocable divorce, and revocable divorce occurs only after a valid marriage. So by ordering a revocable divorce, the master implicitly consents to the marriage.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling and proof regarding the interpretation of a master's statement when his slave marries without permission. The key issue is whether the master's command 'give her talaq' or 'separate from her' constitutes consent (ijazah) to the marriage. The Hanafi ruling is that it does not constitute consent. The text first states the ruling, then provides the Arabic text from the matn, followed by a translation and an extensive explanation. The reasoning is based on the ambiguity of the master's words: they could mean to reject the marriage (since the contract is defective, dissolving it is called talaq) or to give a valid divorce (which would imply a valid marriage exists). The text gives two reasons for preferring the interpretation of rejection: (1) the rebellious nature of the slave, making rejection more appropriate, and (2) the principle that removal (daf') is easier than revocation (raf'), so the master's command is more likely to be removal of the defective contract. It also addresses a potential objection that the literal meaning of talaq is to apply divorce, and it could be interpreted that way. The response is that the contextual indication (the fact that the master is speaking about an unauthorized marriage) overrides the literal meaning. The passage also contrasts this with the situation where the master says 'give a revocable divorce' (talaq raj'i), which does constitute consent because revocable divorce presupposes a valid marriage. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi principle that indications of consent must be clear and unambiguous, and ambiguous statements are construed against the rebel.

Key Terms
اجازة (ijazah): Consent or permission given by the master to the slave's marriage.
طلاق (talaq): Divorce; in this context, it can mean either actual divorce of a valid marriage or dissolution of a defective marriage.
دفع (daf'): Removal or repulsion; here, the act of ending the marriage contract before it is established.
رفع (raf'): Elevation or revocation; here, the act of revoking a marriage after it is validly established.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master tells his slave 'give her one revocable divorce', does this constitute consent to the marriage according to the Hanafi school?
A) No, because the master is ordering a divorce.
B) Yes, because revocable divorce presupposes a valid marriage.
C) It is ambiguous and requires further clarification.
D) Only if the slave obeys the command.

Key Takeaway
A master's ambiguous command after an unauthorised slave marriage is interpreted as rejection, not consent, except when the command specifies a revocable divorce.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Immediate Sale of Slave for Mahr in Fasid Marriage
Intra-Hanafi dispute on scope of permission in marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the immediate sale of a slave for mahr in a fasid marriage, and explain the key proof and counter-proof in their dispute.

Core Idea
Permission for marriage includes both valid and fasid according to Abu Hanifa, but Sahibayn restrict it to valid marriage only.

In this lesson, we examine a classic intra-Hanafi dispute about the scope of a master's permission when he tells his slave to marry. Does permission cover a fasid (defective) marriage, and what happens to the mahr? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, the Sahibayn, differ on this precise point. Following the source, we will trace their proofs and see the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the disputed ruling
The scenario: a master tells his slave 'marry this slave-girl'. The slave marries her in a fasid marriage and consummates it. The dispute: is the slave sold immediately to pay the mahr, or is the mahr taken after he is freed?
The mahr is wajib because the marriage was consummated, even though fasid.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa holds the slave is sold immediately. His proof: the word 'tazawwaj' (marry) is absolute and applies to both valid and fasid marriages. Just as in sale, absolute permission includes fasid sales. Furthermore, a fasid marriage still yields some effects (lineage, mahr, 'iddah).
He argues that the master's permission is unqualified, so it covers any marriage.
3
State Sahibayn's position and their proof
Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold the mahr is taken after freedom. Their proof: the purpose (maqsad) of marriage is chastity (ihsan), which is only achieved through a valid marriage. They analogize to oaths: if one swears not to marry, it applies only to valid marriage, not fasid.
They also argue that the mahr is not a debt on the slave's value because the permission was for a valid marriage, so the master is not liable.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response to the analogy
Abu Hanifa rejects the analogy with oaths because oaths are interpreted according to the swearer's intention, whereas permission is an absolute authorization. Moreover, if a fasid sale conveys ownership (some purpose), so does a fasid marriage convey some effects. The oath analogy is thus inapplicable.
He maintains the absolute wording should be applied generally.
5
Note the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, the slave is sold immediately, which may harm the master's interest (loss of slave). Under Sahibayn, the mahr is deferred, giving the slave time to earn and pay after freedom, protecting the master's property.
The text does not specify which view is mufta bihi; later Hanafi works often prefer Sahibayn's view to protect the master's rights.

Source Text

ومن قال لعبده تزوج هذه الامة فتزوجهانكا حافاسد او دخل بها فانه يباع في المهر عند ابي حنيفة رحمه الله وقالا يؤخذ منه اذا عتق واصله ان الاذن فى النكاح ينتظم الفاسد والجائز عنده فيكون هذا المهر ظاهرا في حق المولى وعندهما ينصرف الى الجائز لاغير فلايكون ظاهرا فى حق المولى في اخذبه بعد العتاق لهما ان المقصود من النكاح في المستقبل الاعفاف والتحصين وذلك بالجائز ولهذا لو حلف لايتزوج ينصرف الى الجائز بخلاف البيع لان بعض المقاصد حاصل وهو ملك التصرفات وله ان اللفظ مطلق فيجرى على اطلاقه كما فى البيع وبعضُ المقاصد فى النكاح الفاسد حاصل كالنسب ووجوب المهر و العدة على اعتبار وجود الوطى ومسألة اليمين ممنوعة على هذه الطريقة

And whoever said to his slave 'marry this slave-girl', and he married her in a fasid marriage or had intercourse with her, then he will be sold for the mahr according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said that the mahr will be taken from him when he is freed. And the basis is that permission for marriage includes both fasid and valid marriage in his (Abu Hanifa's) view, so this mahr becomes apparent against the master's right. In their view, it applies only to the valid marriage, so it is not apparent against the master's right, and thus it is taken after freedom. Their proof is that the future purpose of marriage is to preserve chastity and protect oneself from the unlawful, and that is achieved through a valid marriage; for this reason, if one swears not to marry, the oath applies to a valid marriage. This is different from sale, because some purposes are achieved (in a fasid sale), namely ownership of disposal. And his (Abu Hanifa's) proof is that the word is absolute and is applied according to its absoluteness, as in sale. And some purposes in a fasid marriage are achieved, like lineage, obligation of mahr, and waiting period ('iddah) considering the existence of intercourse. And the case of oath is refuted by this method. Translation... Explanation: ... The scenario is that the master gave permission to his slave with the words 'marry this slave-girl'. Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (known as the Sahibayn). The issue is: if a master tells his slave 'marry this slave-girl' and the slave enters into a fasid (defective) marriage with her and consummates it, what is the ruling regarding payment of the mahr? According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the slave is sold to pay the mahr immediately, because the master's absolute permission includes both valid and fasid marriages. Therefore, the mahr is a debt on the slave's value that the master is liable for (since he permitted the marriage). According to the Sahibayn, the mahr is not payable until the slave is freed; after freedom, it is taken from him. They argue that the purpose of marriage in the future (i.e., the master's intention) is to achieve chastity and protection from sin through a valid marriage, not a fasid one. They support this with an analogy: if someone swears not to marry, the oath applies only to a valid marriage. Similarly, the master's permission should be understood as permission for a valid marriage only. Abu Hanifa responds that the wording is absolute and should be applied generally, just as in sale a fasid sale achieves some purposes (like ownership of disposal). In a fasid marriage, some effects still occur (like lineage, obligation of mahr, and 'iddah). He also rejects the analogy with oaths, noting it is not applicable on this basis. The commentary adds that the specification of a slave-girl in the text is incidental; the same ruling applies to a free woman, and for a specific or unspecified slave-girl.

Key Terms
نکاح فاسد (nikah fasid): A defective marriage that lacks some essential condition but is not invalid (batil); it produces some legal effects like mahr and 'iddah if consummated.
ظاهر في حق المولى (zahir fi haqq al-mawla): A debt that is apparent and collectible from the slave's value, affecting the master's right.
یمین (yamin): An oath; here used as an analogy to interpret the scope of permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The slave is sold immediately to pay the mahr.
B) The mahr is taken from the slave after he is freed.
C) The mahr is not wajib because the marriage is defective.
D) The master must pay the mahr.

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute, the scope of absolute permission for marriage is debated: Abu Hanifa applies it generally to include fasid marriage, while Sahibayn restrict it to valid marriage based on purpose, affecting the timing of mahr payment.

Master's Permission for Slave's Marriage: The Fasid Marriage and Mahr Liability Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on slave sold for mahr if he contracts fasid marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the liability for mahr when a slave contracts a fasid marriage with the master's permission, and explain the qiyas basis for each position.

Core Idea
The scope of permission determines liability: Abu Hanifa includes fasid marriage via absolute wording; Sahibayn restrict to valid based on purpose.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about the consequences of a fasid (invalid) marriage by a slave who was given permission by his master. The core question is whether the master's permission (idhn) extends to invalid marriages or only valid ones. The answer determines whether the slave can be sold to pay the mahr (Abu Hanifa's view) or whether the mahr is taken from the slave after he is freed (Sahibayn's view). The dispute hinges on two different analogies: one with permission for sale (idhn bil-bay') and one with prohibition of marriage (nahi al-nikah) or oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the disputed issue.
Read the passage to understand the scenario: a master tells his slave to marry a specific slave-girl; the slave contracts a fasid marriage and consummates. The dispute is about who bears the mahr liability – the master (by selling the slave) or the slave (after freedom).
The mahr is wajib due to consummation; the ikhtilaf is only about the source of payment.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and his dalil.
Abu Hanifa holds that the slave is sold to pay the mahr. His evidence: the word 'tazawwaj' is absolute and must be applied generally, just as permission for sale (idhn bil-bay') includes both valid and invalid sale. Additionally, some purposes of marriage (lineage, mahr, 'iddah) are achieved even in fasid marriage.
The absolute wording (itlaq al-lafz) is key to his qiyas.
3
State the position of the Sahibayn and their dalil.
The Sahibayn hold that the mahr is taken from the slave after he is freed. Their evidence: the purpose of marriage (chastity and protection) is only achieved through valid marriage, so the permission cannot include fasid marriage. They analogize to an oath not to marry, which is only violated by a valid marriage.
Their argument relies on the maqsad (purpose) of the contract.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response to the Sahibayn's analogy.
Abu Hanifa's supporters counter that purposes like lineage, mahr, and 'iddah are achieved in fasid marriage just as some purposes (ownership of dispositions) are achieved in fasid sale. They reject the analogy with oaths as qiyas ma'a al-fariq because oaths depend on custom, while marriage does not.
The Hanafi response uses a higher-level qiyas to rebut the Sahibayn's specific analogy.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi position.
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the master loses the slave immediately (sold to pay mahr); under Sahibayn, the master avoids immediate loss and the slave pays after freedom. The later relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of the Sahibayn, as it is more cautious regarding the master's property.
This shows how ikhtilaf affects practical outcomes even within the same school.

Source Text
