ومن قال لعبده تزوج هذه الامة فتزوجها نكاحا فاسدا او دخل بها فانه يباع في المهر عند ابي حنيفة رحمه الله وقالا يؤخذ منه اذا عتق واصله ان الاذن فى النكاح ينتظم الفاسد والجائز عنده فيكون هذا المهر ظاهرا في حق المولى وعندهما ينصرف الى الجائز لاغير فلايكون ظاهرا فى حق المولى في اخذبه بعد العتاق لهما ان المقصود من النكاح في المستقبل الاعفاف والتحصين وذلك بالجائز ولهذا لو حلف لايتزوج ينصرف الى الجائز بخلاف البيع لان بعض المقاصد حاصل وهو ملك التصرفات وله ان اللفظ مطلق فيجرى على اطلاقه كما فى البيع وبعضُ المقاصد فى النكاح الفاسد حاصل كالنسب ووجوب المهر و العدة على اعتبار وجود الوطى ومسألة اليمين ممنوعة على هذه الطريقة

ترجمہ اور جس شخص نے اپنے غلام سے کہا۔ اس باندی سے نکاح کرلے۔ اس غلام نے اس باندی سے نکاح فاسد کیا اور اس کے ساتھ دخول کیا تو اس غلام کو مہر میں فروخت کر دیا جائے گا ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک۔ اور صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ غلام سے مہر لیا جائے گا جب وہ آزاد کیا جائے اور ابو حنیفہ کی اصل یہ ہے کہ نکاح میں اجازت نکاح فاسد اور جائز ہر دو کو شامل ہے۔ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک ۔ پس یہ مہر مولی کے حق میں ظاہر نہیں ہوگا۔ لہذا مبر آزادی کے بعد لیا جائے گا۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ آئندہ زمانہ میں نکاح سے مقصود عفت حاصل کرنا اور نفس کو حرام سے بچانا ہے۔ اور یہ ( مقصود ) نکاح جائز سے ( حاصل ہوگا) اور اسی وجہ سے اگر قسم کھائی کہ نکاح نہیں کرے گا تو یہ قسم نکاح جائز کی طرف لوٹے گی ۔ بخلاف بیع کے کیونکہ بعض مقاصد (بیع فاسد سے ) حاصل ہیں اور وہ تصرفات کا مالک ہونا ہے۔ اور امام صاحب کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ لفظ مطلق ہے اپنے اطلاق پر جاری کیا جائے گا۔ جیسا کہ بیع اور بعض مقاصد نکاح فاسد میں بھی حاصل ہیں۔ جیسے نسب، وجوب مہر اور عدت وجود ولی کا اعتبار کرتے ہوئے اور مسئلہ یمین اس طریقہ پر ممنوع ہے۔ تشریح متن میں اشارہ اور امتہ کی قید اتفاقی ہے۔ اسلئے کہ جو کم باندی کا ہے وہی آزاد عورت کا ہے۔ اسی طرح جو حکم معینہ کا ہے وہی غیر معینہ کا ہے۔

صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ مولیٰ نے اپنے غلام کو تزوج هذه الامة الفاظ کے ساتھ نکاح کی اجازت دی۔ غلام نے اس باندی سے نکاح فاسد کیا اور دخول بھی کر لیا تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک مہر ادا کرنے کیلئے اس غلام کو فروخت کر دیا جائے گا۔ اور صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ غلام کو مہر کے سلسلہ میں فروخت نہیں کیا جائے گا بلکہ آزاد ہونے کے بعد غلام سے مہر وصول کیا جائے۔ اختلاف کی بنیاد یہ ہے کہ اجازت نکاح امام صاحب کے نزدیک نکاح صحیح اور نکاح فاسد دونوں کو شامل ہوتی ہے۔ اور صاحبین کے نزدیک صرف نکاح صحیح کو شامل ہوگی نکاح فاسد کو نہیں۔ تو چونکہ نکاح فاسد امام صاحب کے نزدیک مولی کی اجازت سے ہے۔ لہذا مہر مولیٰ کے حق میں ظاہر ہوگا اور غلام کو فروخت کر دیا جائے گا۔ اور چونکہ صاحبین کے نزدیک نکاح فاسد مولی کی اجازت سے نہیں ہے۔ لہذا مہر بھی مولیٰ کے حق میں ظاہر نہیں ہو گا۔ بلکہ غلام کے آزاد ہونے کے بعد اس سے مہر وصول کیا جائے گا۔

صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ نکاح کا مقصود عفت اور پاک دامنی کو حاصل کرنا اور نفس کو فعل حرام سے بچانا ہے۔ اور یہ مقصود نکاح صحیح سے حاصل ہوتا ہے۔ کیونکہ نکاح فاسد کی صورت میں ولی کرنا حرام ہے۔ لہذا مولی کی جانب سے اجازت نکاح صحیح کو شامل ہوگی نہ کہ نکاح فاسد کو ۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ نکاح نہیں کروں گا تو یہ تم نکاح صحیح کو شامل ہوگی ۔ نکاح فاسد کی وجہ سے حانث نہیں ہو گا۔ ہاں البتہ اگر مولی نے اپنے غلام کو بیع کی اجازت دی تو یہ اجازت بھی صحیح اور بیع فاسد دونوں کو شامل ہوگی۔ کیونکہ بعض مقاصد بیع فاسد سے بھی حاصل ہو جاتے ہیں۔ مثلاً تصرفات کا مالک ہونا ، جیسے اعتاق اور ہیں۔

امام صاحب کی دلیل کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ لفظ نتزوج مطلق ہے۔ اور قاعدہ ہے کہ مطلق جب بولا جائے تو اس کو اطلاق پر جاری کیا جاتا ہے۔ لہذا اس لفظ مطلق کو بھی اس کے اطلاق پر جاری کیا جائے گا۔ اور اس کا حکم نکاح صحیح اور نکاح فاسد دونوں کو عام ہوگا جس طرح امر بالبیچ بیچ صحیح اور بیع فاسد دونوں کو شامل ہوتا ہے اور صاحبین کا یہ کہنا کہ بیع فاسد سے بعض مقاصد حاصل ہو جاتے ہیں۔ ہم جواب دیں گے کہ نکاح فاسد سے بھی بعض مقاصد حاصل ہوتے ہیں۔ مثلاً بچہ پیدا ہو جائے تو باپ سے ثبوت نسب ہو گا۔ واطی پر مہر واجب ہوگا اور اگر وطی کی گئی تو عدت واجب ہوگی۔

و مسئلة اليمين ممنوعہ سے صاحبین کے قیاس کا جواب ہے۔ حاصل جواب یہ ہے کہ ہمیں یہ تسلیم نہیں کہ اگر کوئی نکاح نہ کرنے کی قسم کھائے تو یہ ستم نکاح صحیح کو شامل ہوگی نکاح فاسد کو نہیں۔ بلکہ مسیح یہ ہے کہ یہ تم دونوں کو شامل ہے جس طرح نکاح صحیح سے حانث ہو گا اسی طرح نکاح فاسد سے بھی حانث ہوگا۔ اور اگر تھوڑی دیر کیلئے تسلیم کرلیا جائے کہ یہ تم نکاح صحیح کو شامل ہے نکاح فاسد کو نہیں تو امام صاحب کی طرف سے جواب یہ ہو گا کہ نکاح کو قسم پر قیاس کرنا قیاس مع الفارق ہے۔ کیونکہ قسم کا مدار عرف اور رواج پر ہے۔ اس کے برخلاف نکاح کہ اس کا مدار عرف پر نہیں ہے۔ لہذا یہ قیاس بھی درست نہیں ہے۔

فریقین کے دلائل اختصار کے ساتھ یوں بھی بیان کئے جاسکتے ہیں۔ کہ اذن بالبیع بالا تفاق بیع صحیح اور بیع فاسد دونوں کو شامل ہوتا ہے اور تو کیل بالنکاح بالا اتفاق نکاح صحیح کو شامل ہوتا ہے نکاح فاسد کو نہیں ۔ پس صاحبین نے اذن بالنکاح کو قیاس کیا تو کیل بالنکاح پر اور امام صاحب اذن بالنکاح کو قیاس کرتے ہیں اذن بالبیع پر ۔
And whoever said to his slave, 'Marry this slave-girl,' and he married her with an invalid marriage or had intercourse with her, then he (the slave) is sold for the dower according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him). And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It is taken from him when he is freed. And its basis is that permission in marriage includes both the invalid and the valid according to him, so this dower becomes apparent in right of the master, whereas according to them it is restricted to the valid only, so it is not apparent in right of the master to take it after freedom. Their evidence is that the purpose of marriage in the future is chastity and protection, and that is achieved by valid (marriage). For this reason, if one swears not to marry, it is restricted to valid marriage, unlike sale because some purposes are achieved (by invalid sale), namely ownership of dispositions. And his (Abu Hanifa's) evidence is that the word is absolute and is applied according to its absoluteness, as in sale; and some purposes in invalid marriage are achieved, such as lineage, obligation of dower, and 'iddah considering the existence of intercourse. And the issue of the oath is prevented on this method. Translation: And the person who said to his slave, 'Marry this slave-girl,' and the slave performed an invalid marriage with her and had intercourse with her, then this slave will be sold for the dower according to Abu Hanifa. And the two companions said: The dower will be taken from the slave when he is freed. And Abu Hanifa's principle is that permission in marriage includes both invalid and valid according to Imam Abu Hanifa. So this dower will not be apparent in right of the master; therefore it will be taken after freedom. The two companions' evidence is that in the future time, the purpose of marriage is to obtain chastity and to protect the soul from forbidden acts, and this (purpose) is obtained by valid marriage. For this reason, if one swears not to marry, this oath will revert to valid marriage, unlike sale because some purposes (from invalid sale) are obtained, namely ownership of dispositions. And Imam Abu Hanifa's evidence is that the word is absolute and will be applied according to its absoluteness, as in sale; and some purposes in invalid marriage are also obtained, such as lineage, obligation of dower, and 'iddah considering the existence of intercourse. And the issue of the oath is prevented on this method. Explanation: The indication in the text and the restriction to 'this slave-girl' is incidental, because the ruling for a female slave is the same as for a free woman. Similarly, the ruling for a specific (slave-girl) is the same as for an unspecified one. The case is that the master gave permission to his slave with the words 'marry this slave-girl'. The slave performed an invalid marriage with this slave-girl and also had intercourse, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa this slave will be sold to pay the dower. And the two companions say that the slave will not be sold for the dower, but after he is freed, the dower will be collected from the slave. The basis of the difference is that permission for marriage, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, includes both valid and invalid marriage, whereas according to the two companions it only includes valid marriage, not invalid. So since invalid marriage is with the master's permission according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the dower is apparent in right of the master and the slave will be sold. And since invalid marriage is not with the master's permission according to the two companions, the dower is not apparent in right of the master, but the dower will be collected from the slave after he is freed. The two companions' evidence is that the purpose of marriage is to obtain chastity and purity and to protect the soul from forbidden acts, and this purpose is achieved by valid marriage, because in the case of invalid marriage, intercourse is forbidden. Therefore the master's permission includes only valid marriage, not invalid. This is why if someone swears not to marry, the oath includes valid marriage, and he does not become sinful by invalid marriage. However, if the master gave permission to his slave for sale, this permission includes both valid and invalid sale, because some purposes are achieved by invalid sale, such as ownership of dispositions, like manumission. The summary of Imam Abu Hanifa's evidence is that the word 'marry' is absolute, and the rule is that when an absolute expression is uttered, it is applied according to its absoluteness. Therefore this absolute word will also be applied according to its absoluteness, and its ruling will be general for both valid and invalid marriage, just as the command for sale includes both valid and invalid sale. And to the two companions' statement that some purposes are achieved by invalid sale, we respond that some purposes are also achieved by invalid marriage, for example, if a child is born, lineage from the father is established; the dower is obligatory for the one who had intercourse; and if intercourse occurred, the 'iddah becomes obligatory. And the issue of the oath is prevented; this is the response to the two companions' analogy. The summary of the response is that we do not concede that if someone swears not to marry, the oath includes only valid marriage and not invalid. Rather the matter is that the oath includes both, just as one becomes sinful by valid marriage, similarly one becomes sinful by invalid marriage. And if one concedes for a moment that the oath includes only valid marriage and not invalid, then from Imam Abu Hanifa's side the response is that analogizing marriage to an oath is an analogy with a difference, because the basis of an oath is custom and practice, whereas marriage is not based on custom. Therefore this analogy is also not correct. The arguments of both parties can also be briefly stated as follows: Permission for sale by consensus includes both valid and invalid sale, while prohibition of marriage by consensus includes valid marriage, not invalid. So the two companions analogize permission for marriage to prohibition of marriage, and Imam Abu Hanifa analogizes permission for marriage to permission for sale.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the liability for mahr (dower) when a master permits his slave to marry a specific slave-girl, but the slave contracts a fasid (invalid) marriage and consummates it. The core issue is whether the master's permission (idhn) extends to invalid marriages or only valid ones. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the permission covers both valid and invalid marriages because the wording 'tazawwaj' is absolute and should be applied generally, just as permission for sale (idhn bil-bay') includes both valid and invalid sales. Consequently, the mahr becomes a liability on the master, and the slave may be sold to pay it. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) restrict the permission to valid marriages only, arguing that the primary purpose of marriage is 'iffah (chastity) and tahsin (protection from sin), which is only achieved through a valid marriage. Invalid marriage does not serve this purpose, so the permission cannot be understood to include it. They draw an analogy with an oath not to marry, which is only violated by a valid marriage, not an invalid one. They also note that even though some purposes of sale are achieved in an invalid sale (e.g., ownership of the item for transactions), nothing similar is achieved in an invalid marriage, so the analogy with sale fails. The text then elaborates the arguments, with Abu Hanifa's supporters countering that purposes such as establishing lineage, obligating mahr, and observing 'iddah are also achieved in invalid marriage, and they reject the analogy with oaths as qiyas ma'a al-fariq (analogy with a difference), since oaths depend on custom while marriage does not. The passage concludes by summarizing that both sides use qiyas but on different bases: the Sahibayn analogize idhn bil-nikah to nahil al-nikah (prohibition of marriage), while Abu Hanifa analogizes it to idhn bil-bay' (permission for sale). This is a classic example of how Hanafi jurists use the principles of absolute wording (itlaq al-lafz) and consideration of purpose (maqsad) to derive rulings.

Key Terms
اذن (idhn): Permission granted by the master to the slave to perform an action; in this context, permission to marry. The scope of idhn (whether it covers valid and invalid acts) is the point of dispute.
نکاح فاسد (nikah fasid): An invalid marriage that is defective in some essential condition (e.g., missing witnesses, but without permanent prohibition). It produces some legal effects like lineage and 'iddah upon consummation.
مہر (mahr): The dower or bridal gift that is obligatory upon the husband in a valid or consummated invalid marriage. In this passage, the slave owes mahr, and the dispute is about who pays it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), why does the master's permission to marry not include a fasid marriage?
A) Because the word 'tazawwaj' is legally restricted to valid marriages only.
B) Because the purpose of marriage (chastity and protection) is only achieved by a valid marriage, and a fasid marriage does not serve that purpose.
C) Because the master explicitly excluded fasid marriage in his permission.
D) Because the slave acted without the master's consent.

Key Takeaway
The scope of the master's permission for marriage is contested: Abu Hanifa's view extends to fasid marriage through absolute wording and qiyas to sale, while the Sahibayn restrict it to valid marriage based on purpose, and the later mufta bihi follows the Sahibayn.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
12 min
Master's Permission for Invalid Marriage: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on master's permission for fasid nikah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether master's permission covers fasid nikah, and explain their proofs and the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Absolute permission covers both valid and invalid unless purpose restricts it.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). At stake is a practical question: if a master allows his slave to marry and the slave contracts an invalid (fasid) marriage, who bears the financial burden of the mahr? The answer hinges on whether the word 'permission' includes only valid marriages or both valid and invalid. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply Hanafi usul principles—particularly the treatment of absolute language and analogical reasoning (qiyas).

Guided Path
1
Identify the practical consequence
Note the concrete outcome: according to Abu Hanifa, the slave can be sold to pay the mahr; according to the Sahibayn, the mahr is due only after the slave is freed. This difference (thamarah al-khilaf) drives the entire dispute.
The mahr is a debt; who bears it depends on whether the permission extended to the fasid nikah.
2
State the core dispute
The fundamental disagreement is whether the master's permission (idhn bi al-nikah) covers both valid (sahih) and invalid (fasid) marriage. Abu Hanifa says yes; the Sahibayn say no.
This is a dispute about the scope of a general permission.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn argue that the purpose of marriage is chastity, which is only achieved by valid marriage. They draw analogies to oaths (yamin) and authorization (tawkil) where only valid acts are intended.
They rely on purposive interpretation (maqasid) and precedent in similar constructs.
4
Analyze Imam Abu Hanifa's proof
Imam Abu Hanifa argues from the absolute wording of 'tazawwuj'— it remains general unless restricted. He also notes that fasid nikah produces legal effects (lineage, mahr, 'iddah), fulfilling some purposes. He analogizes permission for marriage to permission for sale (idhn bi al-bay'), which covers both valid and invalid sales by consensus.
He rejects the Sahibayn's analogy to oaths as qiyas ma'a al-fariq (analogy with a difference) because oaths are custom-dependent, marriage is not.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the master is liable for the mahr and may sell the slave. Under the Sahibayn, the slave owes the mahr personally, collectible only after freedom.
Both views are presented; the text does not definitively choose one, but understanding both is essential for fatwa.

نكاح فاسد کیا اور دخول بھی کر لیا تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک مہر ادا کرنے کیلئے اس غلام کو فروخت کر دیا جائے گا۔ اور صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ غلام کو مہر کے سلسلہ میں فروخت نہیں کیا جائے گا بلکہ آزاد ہونے کے بعد غلام سے مہر وصول کیا جائے۔ اختلاف کی بنیاد یہ ہے کہ اجازت نکاح امام صاحب کے نزدیک نکاح صحیح اور نکاح فاسد دونوں کو شامل ہوتی ہے۔ اور صاحبین کے نزدیک صرف نکاح صحیح کو شامل ہوگی نکاح فاسد کو نہیں۔ تو چونکہ نکاح فاسد امام صاحب کے نزدیک مولی کی اجازت سے ہے۔ لہذا مہر مولیٰ کے حق میں ظاہر ہوگا اور غلام کو فروخت کر دیا جائے گا۔ اور چونکہ صاحبین کے نزدیک نکاح فاسد مولی کی اجازت سے نہیں ہے۔ لہذا مہر بھی مولیٰ کے حق میں ظاہر نہیں ہو گا۔ بلکہ غلام کے آزاد ہونے کے بعد اس سے مہر وصول کیا جائے گا۔
صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ نکاح کا مقصود عفت اور پاک دامنی کو حاصل کرنا اور نفس کو فعل حرام سے بچانا ہے۔ اور یہ مقصود نکاح صحیح سے حاصل ہوتا ہے۔ کیونکہ نکاح فاسد کی صورت میں ولی کرنا حرام ہے۔ لہذا مولی کی جانب سے اجازت نکاح صحیح کو شامل ہوگی نہ کہ نکاح فاسد کو ۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ نکاح نہیں کروں گا تو یہ تم نکاح صحیح کو شامل ہوگی ۔ نکاح فاسد کی وجہ سے حانث نہیں ہو گا۔ ہاں البتہ اگر مولی نے اپنے غلام کو بیع کی اجازت دی تو یہ اجازت بھی صحیح اور بیع فاسد دونوں کو شامل ہوگی۔ کیونکہ بعض مقاصد بیع فاسد سے بھی حاصل ہو جاتے ہیں۔ مثلاً تصرفات کا مالک ہونا ، جیسے اعتاق اور ہیں۔
امام صاحب کی دلیل کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ لفظ نتزوج مطلق ہے۔ اور قاعدہ ہے کہ مطلق جب بولا جائے تو اس کو اطلاق پر جاری کیا جاتا ہے۔ لہذا اس لفظ مطلق کو بھی اس کے اطلاق پر جاری کیا جائے گا۔ اور اس کا حکم نکاح صحیح اور نکاح فاسد دونوں کو عام ہوگا جس طرح امر بالبیع بیع صحیح اور بیع فاسد دونوں کو شامل ہوتا ہے اور صاحبین کا یہ کہنا کہ بیع فاسد سے بعض مقاصد حاصل ہو جاتے ہیں۔ ہم جواب دیں گے کہ نکاح فاسد سے بھی بعض مقاصد حاصل ہوتے ہیں۔ مثلاً بچہ پیدا ہو جائے تو باپ سے ثبوت نسب ہو گا۔ واطی پر مہر واجب ہوگا اور اگر وطی کی گئی تو عدت واجب ہوگی۔
و مسئلة اليمين ممنوعہ سے صاحبین کے قیاس کا جواب ہے۔ حاصل جواب یہ ہے کہ ہمیں یہ تسلیم نہیں کہ اگر کوئی نکاح نہ کرنے کی قسم کھائے تو یہ ستم نکاح صحیح کو شامل ہوگی نکاح فاسد کو نہیں۔ بلکہ مسیح یہ ہے کہ یہ تم دونوں کو شامل ہے جس طرح نکاح صحیح سے حانث ہو گا اسی طرح نکاح فاسد سے بھی حانث ہوگا۔ اور اگر تھوڑی دیر کیلئے تسلیم کرلیا جائے کہ یہ تم نکاح صحیح کو شامل ہے نکاح فاسد کو نہیں تو امام صاحب کی طرف سے جواب یہ ہو گا کہ نکاح کو قسم پر قیاس کرنا قیاس مع الفارق ہے۔ کیونکہ قسم کا مدار عرف اور رواج پر ہے۔ اس کے برخلاف نکاح کہ اس کا مدار عرف پر نہیں ہے۔ لہذا یہ قیاس بھی درست نہیں ہے۔
فریقین کے دلائل اختصار کے ساتھ یوں بھی بیان کئے جاسکتے ہیں۔ کہ اذن بالبیع بالا تفاق بیع صحیح اور بیع فاسد دونوں کو شامل ہوتا ہے اور التوكيل بالنكاح بالا اتفاق نکاح صحیح کو شامل ہوتا ہے نکاح فاسد کو نہیں ۔ پس صاحبین نے اذن بالنكاح کو قیاس کیا التوكيل بالنكاح پر اور امام صاحب اذن بالنكاح کو قیاس کرتے ہیں اذن بالبیع پر ۔
If a slave entered into an invalid (fasid) marriage and also had intercourse, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, that slave will be sold to pay the mahr. The Sahibayn say that the slave will not be sold for the mahr, but the mahr will be collected from the slave after he becomes free. The basis of the dispute is that permission for marriage, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, covers both valid and invalid marriage. According to the Sahibayn, it covers only valid marriage, not invalid. So since the invalid marriage is with the master's permission according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the mahr becomes due to the master, and the slave will be sold. And since the invalid marriage is not with the master's permission according to the Sahibayn, the mahr does not become due to the master; rather, it will be collected from the slave after he becomes free.
The proof of the Sahibayn is that the purpose of marriage is to attain chastity and protect the soul from forbidden acts, and this purpose is achieved by valid marriage. Because in an invalid marriage, intercourse is forbidden. Therefore, the master's permission covers only valid marriage, not invalid. This is the reason that if someone swears an oath that 'I will not marry,' the oath covers only valid marriage; he does not become sinful by invalid marriage. However, if the master permitted his slave to make a sale (bay'), this permission covers both valid and invalid sale, because some purposes are fulfilled even by an invalid sale, such as ownership of actions like emancipation.
The gist of Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that the word 'tazawwuj' (marriage) is absolute, and the rule is that when an absolute word is used, it remains on its absoluteness. Therefore, this absolute word will be applied to its absolute meaning, and its ruling applies to both valid and invalid marriage, just as the command for sale includes both valid and invalid sale. And as for the Sahibayn's statement that an invalid sale fulfills some purposes, we reply that an invalid marriage also fulfills some purposes, e.g., if a child is born, lineage from the father is established, mahr becomes obligatory on the one who had intercourse, and if intercourse occurred, 'iddah becomes obligatory.
As for the issue of the oath (yamin) which they objected to, that is a response to the Sahibayn's qiyas. The gist of the response is that we do not accept that if someone swears an oath not to marry, the oath covers only valid marriage and not invalid. Rather, the correct view is that it covers both, just as he becomes sinful by valid marriage, so too he becomes sinful by invalid marriage. And if one concedes temporarily that the oath covers only valid marriage, then the response from Imam Abu Hanifa is that analogizing marriage to an oath is qiyas ma'a al-fariq (analogy with a difference), because an oath depends on custom and practice, unlike marriage which does not depend on custom. Therefore, this analogy is not correct.
The proofs of the two sides can also be concisely stated: Permission for sale (idhn bi al-bay') by consensus covers both valid and invalid sale, while authorization for marriage (tawkil bi al-nikah) by consensus covers only valid marriage, not invalid. So the Sahibayn analogized permission for marriage (idhn bi al-nikah) to authorization for marriage (tawkil bi al-nikah), while Imam Abu Hanifa analogizes permission for marriage to permission for sale (idhn bi al-bay').
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The issue is whether a master's permission for his slave to marry includes permission for an invalid (fasid) marriage. The practical consequence concerns the mahr: if the slave contracts a fasid nikah with intercourse, can the master sell the slave to pay the mahr? According to Imam Abu Hanifa, yes, because the permission covers fasid nikah, so the mahr is due on the master's property. The Sahibayn say no, because permission only covers valid nikah, so the mahr is a debt on the slave, to be collected only after he is freed. The Sahibayn argue that the purpose of marriage is chastity, only achieved by valid nikah, and they compare to oaths (yamin) which only apply to valid marriage. Imam Abu Hanifa counters that the word 'tazawwuj' is absolute, so it applies to both valid and invalid. He also notes that an invalid marriage still has legal effects (lineage, mahr, 'iddah). He rejects the analogy to oaths because oaths depend on custom while marriage does not. The author summarizes that the Sahibayn analogize permission to marry (idhn bi al-nikah) to authorization (tawkil), while Imam Abu Hanifa analogizes it to permission for sale (idhn bi al-bay'), which by consensus covers both valid and invalid. The text does not explicitly resolve which view is mufta bihi, but it presents both sides thoroughly.

Key Terms
اذن بالنكاح (idhn bi al-nikah): Master's permission for a slave to marry, seen as authority to contract a marriage that binds the master's property.
صاحبین (Sahibayn): Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two leading students of Imam Abu Hanifa.
نكاح فاسد (nikah fasid): An invalid marriage that may have some legal effects (e.g., mahr, lineage) but is defective due to missing a condition.
قیاس مع الفارق (qiyas ma'a al-fariq): An analogy that is invalid because there is a significant difference between the two cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, if a slave with master's permission contracts a fasid nikah and has intercourse, how is the mahr to be paid?
A) The slave is sold to pay the mahr.
B) The master pays the mahr directly.
C) The mahr is due only after the slave is freed.
D) The mahr is waived because the marriage is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The scope of a master's permission for marriage is disputed within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa includes fasid nikah, the Sahibayn exclude it, affecting who bears the mahr.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Ruling on Mahr of Indebted Slave's Wife: Sharing with Creditors
Ruling and proof for wife sharing mahr with creditors of indebted slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the mahr of an indebted slave's wife and explain the qiyas basis that makes her equal to creditors.

Core Idea
The wife's mahr competes equally with other creditors in the slave's insolvent estate.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a delicate intersection of marriage, debt, and slavery. A master marries his indebted slave (who is permitted to trade) to a woman for her fair mahr. The slave already owes debts to other creditors. Does the wife's mahr enjoy priority, or must she share? The Hanafi ruling is clear: she shares equally with the creditors. The reasoning employs a powerful analogy to a debt of consumption, establishing that the mahr debt is unavoidable once the marriage is valid, just like a liability for destroyed property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its key condition
First, locate the ruling: the marriage is valid, and the woman is an equal partner with the creditors regarding her mahr. Note the condition: the marriage must be for the fair mahr (mahr al-mithl). Why is this condition important? Because if a specific mahr were agreed upon, it might affect the analysis, but the text assumes mahr al-mithl to highlight the general principle.
The condition of mahr al-mithl prevents the parties from artificially inflating the mahr to circumvent creditors.
2
Understand the primary analogy (qiyas)
The author compares the mahr debt to a debt of consumption (dayn al-istihlak). If the slave destroys someone's property, the victim becomes a creditor sharing equally with others. Similarly, the mahr becomes due through an unavoidable cause (the master's authority), so the wife is treated like any other creditor. Also, the author analogizes to an indebted sick person (marid madhyun) who marries: the wife's mahr shares with existing creditors. This shows the principle is not unique to slaves.
The analogy to the sick debtor reinforces that the ruling is based on the debtor's financial state, not the slave's status.
3
Analyze the response to the objection
An objection is raised: the master's action of marrying the slave nullifies the creditors' rights, which is not allowed. The response distinguishes between intentional nullification and incidental effect. Marriage pertains to the person (adam), not directly to property (mal). Thus, any reduction in creditors' recovery is unintended and legally permissible. The ruling is established because the cause (master's authority) is present and no preventive factor (mani') exists.
This distinction between personal and financial domains is a hallmark of Hanafi legal reasoning.
4
Apply the ruling to a concrete example
The text provides an example: slave worth 2000 rupees, three creditors each owed 1000, wife's mahr 1000. Total claims = 4000, but value only 2000. Each receives a quarter (500 rupees). The balance can be claimed after the slave is freed. Walk through the arithmetic to see the equal treatment. Then consider if the slave's value were 3000: each would get 3000/4 = 750, leaving a remainder.
The example assumes the slave is sold; the proceeds are distributed proportionally (pari passu).

ومن زوج عبدا مدیوناما ذو ناله امرأة جاز والمرأة اسوة للغرماء فى مهرها و معناه اذا كان النكاح بمهر المثل ووجهه ان سبب ولاية المولى ملكه الرقبة على ما نذكره والنكاح لايلاقى حق الغرماء بالابطال مقصودا الا انه اذاصح النكاح وجب الدين بسبب لامردله فشابه دین الاستهلاک و صار كالمريض المديون اذا تزوج امرأة فبمهر مثلها اسوة للغرماء

ترجمہ اور جس شخص نے قرض دار ماذون لہ التجارة غلام کا کسی عورت سے نکاح کر دیا تو ( نکاح ) جائز ہے۔ اور عورت اپنے مہر کے بارے میں قرض خواہوں کے ساتھ یکساں برابر کی شریک ہوگی۔ اور اس کی مراد یہ ہے کہ جب نکاح مہر مثل کے عوض ہوا ہو ۔ اور یکساں مستحق ہونے کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ مولیٰ کی ولایت کا سبب مولیٰ کے غلام کی گردن کا مالک ہونا ہے۔ چنانچہ ہم اس کو بیان کریں گے۔ اور نکاح قرض خواہوں کے حق کے ساتھ (اس طرح) متصل نہیں ہوا کہ (ان کے حقوق ) باطل کرنے کا قصد ہومگر یہ کہ جب نکاح صحیح ہو گیا تو قرضہ ایسے سبب سے واجب ہو گیا جس کو رد نہیں کیا جا سکتا ہے۔ پس دین مہر دین استبلاک کے مشابہ ہو گیا اور اس قرضدار مریض کے مانند ہو گیا جب اس کے کسی عورت سے نکاح کیا ( تو یہ عورت اپنے مہر مشکل میں قرض خواہوں کے ساتھ برابر کی حقدار ہوگی ۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ مولیٰ نے اپنے قرضدار ماذون لہ التجارة غلام کا کسی عورت کے ساتھ مہر مثل کے عوض نکاح کیا تو یہ نکاح جائز ہے اور عورت اپنے مہر کے بارے میں قرض خواہوں کے ساتھ برابر کی شریک ہوگی۔ مثلاً غلام دو ہزار روپیہ میں فروخت کیا گیا اور تین قرض خواہ ہیں۔ جن میں ہر ایک کا قرضہ ایک ایک ہزار روپیہ ہے اور چوتھی عورت جس کا مہر ایک ہزار روپیہ ہے۔ پس ان چاروں میں سے ہر ایک چوتھائی قیمت یعنی پانچ سو روپیہ کا مستحق ہوگا۔ اور باقی کا مطالبہ غلام کے آزاد ہونے کے بعد کرسکتا ہے۔ دلیل اس بات کی کہ عورت قرض خواہوں کے ساتھ برابر کی شریک ہوگی ، یہ ہے کہ مقتضی مہر یعنی ولایت مولی موجود ہے اور ولایت مولی اسلئے موجود ہے کہ اس کا سبب متحقق ہے یعنی غلام کی گردن کا مالک ہونا۔ پس جب مولیٰ نے اس کا نکاح کیا تو گویا اس غلام پر مہر لازم کر دیا ۔ لہذا مہر مولی کے حق میں ظاہر ہوگا۔
والنکاح لا یلاقی سے ایک اعتراض کا جواب ہے۔ حاصل اعتراض یہ ہے کہ مولیٰ نے غلام مدیون کا نکاح کر کے قرضخواہوں کے حقوق کو باطل کر دیا ہے۔ اور ابطال حق غیر درست نہیں ہے۔ لہذا عورت قرض خواہوں کے ساتھ برابر کی شریک نہ ہونی چاہئے ۔ جواب نکاح قرض خواہوں کے حقوق کے ساتھ اس طرح متصل نہیں ہوا ہے کہ ان کے حقوق کو باطل کرنے کا قصد کیا گیا ہو۔ کیونکہ نکاح کا مثل آدمیت ہے۔ اور قرض خواہوں کا حق متصل ہے مالیت کے ساتھ زیادہ سے زیادہ یہ کہا جا سکتا ہے کہ قرض خواہوں کا حق بلا قصد ضمنا باطل ہو گیا ہے
لیکن یہ معتبر نہیں ہے۔ حاصل دلیل یہ کہ جب مقتضی تحقیق ہو گیا اور مانع ابطال حق غیر بالقصد) منظمی ہو گیا۔ تو حکم ثابت ہو جائے گا۔ بہر حال جب نکاح درست ہو گیا تو دین مہر ایسے سبب سے واجب ہو گیا جس کو رفع نہیں کیا جا سکتا ہے۔ پس یہ دین مہر دین استبلاک کے مشابہ ہو گیا یعنی عبد مدیون ماذون لہ التجارة نے کسی انسان کا مال ہلاک کر دیا تو صاحب مال قرض خواہوں کے ساتھ برابر کا
And whoever gives in marriage an indebted slave who is allowed to trade (ma'dhun lahu al-tijara) to a woman, it is permissible, and the woman is equal to the creditors regarding her mahr. Its meaning: when the marriage is for the fair mahr (mahr al-mithl). And the face of it: the cause of the master's authority is his ownership of the slave's person (raqabah), as we will mention. And marriage does not connect to the right of the creditors with the intent of nullification, but when the marriage becomes valid, a debt becomes obligatory due to a cause that cannot be rejected, so it resembles a debt of consumption (dayn al-istihlak), and it becomes like the indebted patient (marid madhyun) who marries a woman, so with her fair mahr she is equal to the creditors. Translation: And that person who gave in marriage an indebted slave who is allowed to trade (ma'dhun lahu al-tijara) to a woman, then the marriage is permissible, and the woman will be an equal partner with the creditors regarding her mahr. And its meaning: when the marriage is for the fair mahr. And the reason for equal entitlement is that the cause of the master's authority is that the master is the owner of the slave's person. We will explain that. And marriage did not connect to the creditors' right in such a way that there was intent to nullify them, but when the marriage became valid, the debt became obligatory due to a cause that cannot be rejected. So the debt of mahr resembles a debt of consumption, and it becomes like the indebted patient when he marries a woman: she is equal to the creditors regarding her mahr. Elaboration of the case: The master gave his indebted slave, who is allowed to trade, in marriage to a woman for the fair mahr. This marriage is valid, and the woman will be an equal partner with the creditors regarding her mahr. For example: The slave was sold for two thousand rupees, and there are three creditors, each owed one thousand rupees, and a fourth woman whose mahr is one thousand rupees. So each of these four is entitled to a quarter of the price, i.e., five hundred rupees. And the remainder can be claimed after the slave is freed. The proof that the woman is an equal partner with the creditors is that the requirement (muqtada) of mahr, i.e., the master's authority, is present, and the master's authority is present because its cause is realized: the ownership of the slave's person. So when the master married him, he effectively made the mahr binding on the slave. Therefore, the mahr appears in the master's right. And the phrase 'wal-nikahu la yulaqi' is a response to an objection. The gist of the objection: The master, by marrying the indebted slave, has nullified the creditors' rights, and nullifying another's right is not permissible. Therefore, the woman should not be an equal partner with the creditors. The response: Marriage did not connect to the creditors' right with the intent of nullifying them, because marriage pertains to the person (adamiyya), while creditors' right pertains to property (maliyya). At most, the creditors' right is nullified unintentionally and implicitly, but that is not considered. The gist of the proof: When the requirement (muqtada) is realized and the preventive factor (mani') is absent, the ruling is established. In any case, when the marriage becomes valid, the mahr debt becomes obligatory due to a cause that cannot be removed. This mahr debt resembles a debt of consumption, i.e., if the indebted slave who is allowed to trade destroys someone's property, then the owner of the property is equal with the creditors.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage gives a ruling on the marriage of a slave who is indebted and permitted to trade (ma'dhun lahu al-tijara). If the master marries such a slave to a woman for the fair mahr (mahr al-mithl), the marriage is valid. However, because the slave is already in debt, the wife's right to mahr competes with the rights of other creditors. The ruling is that the wife is treated equally with the other creditors: they all share proportionally in the value of the slave's estate (or proceeds from sale). The author provides an example: slave worth 2000 rupees, three creditors each owed 1000, wife's mahr 1000. Total claims = 4000, but slave's value only 2000, so each gets 500 (a quarter of 2000). The remainder can be claimed after the slave is freed. The reason is that the master's authority (wilayah) over the slave arises from ownership of the slave's person. The marriage does not intentionally aim to nullify creditors' rights; it merely creates a debt that, once established, cannot be rejected, similar to a debt of consumption (e.g., if the slave destroyed someone's property). The author compares the situation to a sick debtor (marid madhyun) who marries: the wife's mahr shares equally with existing creditors. The passage also addresses an objection that the master's action might be seen as nullifying creditors' rights. The response: marriage relates to the person, not directly property, so the nullification is unintentional and implicit; the mahr debt is established because the cause (master's authority) is present and no countervailing preventive factor exists.

Key Terms
ماذون لہ التجارة (ma'dhun lahu al-tijara): A slave who is permitted by the master to engage in trade, thus having some financial autonomy.
مهر مثل (mahr al-mithl): The fair mahr determined by the mahr of women of similar status, not a specified amount.
اسوة للغرماء (uswatun li al-ghurama'): Equal to the creditors, sharing proportionally in the slave's estate.
دین استبلاک (dayn al-istihlak): Debt arising from consumption or destruction of property, i.e., a liability for value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A master marries his indebted slave (ma'dhun) to a woman for mahr al-mithl. The slave has creditors. How is the mahr treated?
A) The mahr is paid in full before other creditors.
B) The wife is an equal partner with the creditors for the mahr.
C) The mahr is not due because the slave is indebted.
D) The master must pay the mahr from his own property.

Key Takeaway
The wife's mahr shares equally with other creditors in the slave's insolvent estate, based on the analogy to unavoidable debts like consumption.

Marriage of Indebted Slave: Wife Equal with Creditors
Ruling on validity of marriage of indebted slave and wife's share in mahr with creditors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the marriage of an indebted ma'dhūn slave and explain the qiyas that places the wife's mahr equal with creditors.

Core Idea
Marriage of an indebted slave is valid, and his wife's mahr shares equally with creditors.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a specific scenario: a master marries his indebted slave (who has permission to trade) to a woman with mahr al-mithl. The ruling affirms the marriage's validity but clarifies that the wife's claim to mahr is not prioritized over other creditors. The reasoning employs a qiyas to a debt of destruction and to the case of a terminally ill debtor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Case
The case involves a master (mawlā) marrying his indebted slave who is permitted to trade (ma'dhūn lahu) to a woman. The slave is in debt from trade.
The slave's permission to trade is key—he can incur debts related to trade.
2
State the Ruling
The marriage is valid (jā'iz), and the wife's mahr is on equal footing (uswah) with the other creditors of the slave.
The wife does not have priority over other creditors; she shares proportionally.
3
Understand the Condition
The ruling applies specifically when the marriage is conducted with mahr al-mithl (appropriate dowry for her status), not when a specified mahr is stipulated that could potentially harm creditors.
The passage explicitly says 'wa ma'nāhu idhā kāna al-nikāh bi-mahr al-mithl'.
4
Analyze the Proof
The reasoning (wajh) is that the mawlā's authority over the slave stems from ownership of the slave's person (raqabah). Marriage does not aim to invalidate creditors' rights. Once valid, the mahr becomes a debt that cannot be repudiated, resembling a debt of destruction (dayn al-istihlak) and the case of a terminally ill debtor who marries—his wife's mahr also shares equally with creditors.
The qiyas is to a situation where the debtor intentionally incurs a debt (marriage) that is unavoidable.
5
Apply the Ruling
In practice, when the slave's assets are distributed among creditors, the wife receives a proportionate share based on the mahr al-mithl, not full priority.
This reinforces the principle that new debts incurred with a valid cause share equally with existing unsecured debts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن زوج عبدا مدیونا مأذونا له امرأة جاز والمرأة اسوة للغرماء فى مهرها و معناه اذا كان النكاح بمهر المثل ووجهه ان سبب ولاية المولى ملكه الرقبة على ما نذكره والنكاح لايلاقى حق الغرماء بالابطال مقصودا الا انه اذاصح النكاح وجب الدين بسبب لامردله فشابه دین الاستهلاک و صار كالمريض المديون اذا تزوج امرأة فبمهر مثلها اسوة للغرماء
And whoever marries a slave who is in debt and permitted (to trade) to a woman, it is permissible, and the woman is equal with the creditors regarding her dowry. Its meaning is when the marriage is with a dowry of the like (mahr al-mithl). The reasoning is that the cause of the guardian's authority is his ownership of the slave's person (raqabah) as we will mention. And marriage does not encounter the right of the creditors with the intent to invalidate it. But when the marriage is valid, the debt becomes obligatory due to a cause that cannot be repudiated. So it resembles the debt of destruction (dayn al-istihlak) and becomes like a sick person in debt who marries a woman, so she is equal with the creditors regarding her mahr al-mithl.

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling of a mawlā marrying his indebted slave who has permission to trade (ma'dhūn lahu al-tijārah) to a woman with a mahr al-mithl. The marriage is valid, and the wife's right to her mahr is equal to the creditors' rights. The key reasoning is that the mawlā's authority over the slave arises from his ownership of the slave's person (raqabah). The marriage does not intentionally invalidate creditors' rights; rather, the mahr becomes a debt that arises from a cause that cannot be rejected, similar to a debt arising from destruction of property (istihlak). The analogy is made to a terminally ill debtor who marries: his wife's mahr al-mithl shares equally with creditors. Thus, the wife and creditors are equal in claiming from the slave's asset value, with the wife's share proportionate to the mahr.

Key Terms
مأذون له (ma'dhūn lahu): A slave granted permission by his master to engage in trade.
اسوة للغرماء (uswah lil-ghuramā'): Equal footing with creditors in sharing the available assets proportionally.
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The appropriate dowry for a woman of her status, not a specified amount.
دین الاستهلاک (dayn al-istihlak): Debt arising from the destruction of another's property (e.g., tortious liability).

Master's Obligation to House His Married Slave-Girl
Ruling on master's housing obligation for married slave-girl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a master is not obliged to house his married slave-girl and explain the conditions under which maintenance becomes due.

Core Idea
A master is not obliged to house his married slave-girl; her maintenance is tied to confinement, not marriage.

This passage clarifies that marriage does not affect the master's rights over his slave-girl. The master's right to service remains intact, and he is under no obligation to provide a separate residence. However, if he chooses to do so, certain financial responsibilities arise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and default ruling
The subject is a master who marries his slave-girl. The default ruling is that he is not obliged to provide her a house with the husband.
The text explicitly states 'it is not upon him to provide her a house'.
2
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
The master's right to service (istikhdam) remains after marriage, and providing a house (tabwi') would invalidate that right because it would release her from confinement.
Think of service as the default; housing is an exception.
3
Examine the effect of tabwi'
If the master does provide a house, the slave-girl becomes entitled to maintenance (nafaqah) and housing (sukna) from the master, because she is now confined from service (ihtibas).
Maintenance is compensation for confinement.
4
Analyze the master's ability to revert
Even after providing a house, the master can still demand service later, because his ownership right persists and is not forfeited by tabwi'.
The text gives the example of marriage: marriage does not forfeit rights.
5
Summarize the mufta bihi
The operative ruling: master's right to service remains; no obligation to house; if housed, maintenance is due; master can still demand service.
This is the relied-upon position from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن زوج امته فليس عليه ان يبوئها بيت الزوج ولكنها تخدم المولى ويقال للزوج متى ظفرت بها وطئتها لان حق المولى فى الاستخدام باق والتبوية ابطال له فان بوأها معه بيتا فلها النفقة والسكنى والافلا لان النفقة تقابل الاحتباس ولوبوأها بيتا تم بداله ان يستخدمها له ذلك لان الحق باق لبقاء الملک فلا يسقط بالتبوية كمالا يسقط بالنكاح
And whoever marries his slave-girl, then it is not upon him to provide her a house with the husband, but she serves the mawlā, and it is said to the husband: whenever you find an opportunity with her, have intercourse with her, because the right of the mawlā to service (istikhdām) remains, and providing a house (tabwī') would invalidate it. If he does provide her a house with him, then she has the right to maintenance (nafaqah) and housing (suknā); otherwise not, because maintenance is in exchange for confinement (ihtibās). And if he provides a house, then if he later decides to let her serve him, he has that right, because the right remains due to the continued ownership, so it is not forfeited by providing a house, just as it is not forfeited by marriage.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation where a master marries his slave-girl to a freeman. The master retains the right to her services, so he is not obliged to move her to the husband's house. The husband may visit her at the master's place. If the master does choose to provide a separate residence, then the slave-girl becomes entitled to maintenance and housing from the master (since she is confined from service). However, even after providing a house, the master can still require her service if he wishes, because his ownership right persists. The principle is that marriage does not diminish the master's ownership rights over the slave-girl.

Key Terms
تبویة (tabwī'): Providing a residence for the slave-girl with her husband.
استخدام (istikhdām): The master's right to demand service from his slave-girl.
احتباس (ihtibās): Confinement of the slave-girl to the master's service, which is the basis for maintenance obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He must provide a house for the couple.
B) He is not required to provide a house but may do so voluntarily.
C) He must provide a house only if the slave-girl demands it.
D) He cannot provide a house because the husband must provide housing.

Key Takeaway
A master is not obliged to house his married slave-girl; maintenance is owed only when she is confined from service by being housed separately, and even then, the master may still demand service.

Master's Authority to Compel Slaves into Marriage: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Dispute on whether a master can compel his male and female slaves into marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on ijbar of slaves in marriage and compare it with the Shafi'i position using the proof structure presented.

Core Idea
A master can compel both male and female slaves into marriage to protect his property from zina.

This passage from al-Hidayah presents the Hanafi argument for compelling slaves into marriage without their consent. It contrasts with Imam al-Shafi'i's view and notes an intra-madhhab narration from Abu Hanifa himself. Understanding this dispute reveals how the Hanafi school uses qiyas and ownership rights to derive rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school rules regarding compulsion of male and female slaves into marriage.
The master can compel both; no consent needed.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
State Imam al-Shafi'i's position and the intra-Hanafi narration that aligns with it.
Shafi'i: no compulsion for male slave; female slave can be compelled due to ownership of usufruct.
3
Analyse the opponent's proof
Explain the reasoning behind the Shafi'i view: marriage is a personal right, and the male slave is property.
Opponent argues that the master does not own the right to contract marriage on behalf of the male slave.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Detail the Hanafi counter-argument: marriage is a rectification of property (islah al-milk) and is analogous ijbar of the female slave via qiyas.
The master's ownership includes authority to arrange marriage for benefit, preventing zina.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical difference in ruling between the two schools.
Hanafi: master can marry off male slave without consent. Shafi'i: consent required for male slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رضى الله عنه ذكر تزويج المولى عبده وامته ولم يذكررضا هما و هذا يرجع الى مذهبنا ان للولى اجبار هما على النكاح وعند الشافعى لا اجبار فى العبدو هو رواية عن ابي حنيفة لان النكاح من خصائص الأدمية والعبد داخل تحت ملك المولى من حيث انه مال فلایملک انکاحه بخلاف الامة لانه مالک منافع بضعها فیملک تمليكها ولنا ان الانكاح اصلاح ملكه لان فيه تحصينه عن الزناء الذي هو سبب الهلاك والنقصان فيملكه اعتبارا بالامة بخلاف المكاتب والمكاتبة لانهما التحقا بالاحرار تصرفا فيشترط رضا هما
He (may Allah be pleased with him) said: The mention of the mawlā marrying his male slave and his slave-girl, and he did not mention their consent. This refers to our madhhab that the guardian has the right to compel both of them into marriage. According to al-Shāfiʿī, there is no compulsion regarding the male slave, and it is also a narration from Abū Ḥanīfah, because marriage is a distinctive characteristic of humanity, and the male slave falls under the ownership of the mawlā in the sense that he is property, so he does not own the authority to marry him off, unlike the slave-girl because he owns the usufruct of her vagina (manāfiʿ al-baḍʿ), so he owns the transfer of that. Our proof is that marriage is a rectification of one’s property because it protects the slave from zina, which is a cause of destruction and loss, so he can do it by analogy with the slave-girl, unlike the mukātab and mukātabah because they have joined the free in terms of disposal, so their consent is required.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage addresses the dispute about a master's authority to compel his slaves into marriage. In the Hanafi madhhab, the master can compel both male and female slaves; the marriage is valid without their explicit consent. Imam al-Shāfiʿī holds that compulsion is not allowed for the male slave because marriage is a personal right and the slave is property; only the female slave can be compelled because the master owns the usufruct of her private part. There is also a narration from Imām Abū Ḥanīfah that aligns with this view. The Hanafi response is that marriage benefits the master by protecting his property from zina and its consequences, so by analogy with the female slave, the male slave can also be compelled. However, mukātab slaves (those under contract of manumission) are exempt because they have joined the free in terms of disposal of property and self-determination.

Key Terms
اجبار (ijbār): The authority of the guardian (here the master) to contract marriage without the consent of the person under his guardianship.
مکاتب (mukātab): A slave who has contracted with his master to earn his freedom by paying installments.
منافع بضع (manāfiʿ al-baḍʿ): The usufruct of the female slave's private part, which the master owns and can transfer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No, only female slaves can be compelled.
B) Yes, because marriage protects the master's property from zina.
C) Only if the slave is a mukātab.
D) Only with the slave's consent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows a master to compel both male and female slaves into marriage based on qiyas and property protection, while the Shafi'i school restricts compulsion to female slaves only.

Master Not Obliged to House Married Slave Girl
Ruling on master's duty to provide accommodation for married slave girl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a master's obligation to provide accommodation for his married slave girl and explain the legal reasoning (ta'lil) based on the master's retained right of service and the principle that nafaqah is in exchange for restraint (ihtibas).

Core Idea
The master's right to service takes precedence; housing the married slave is a voluntary act, not an obligation.

This lesson examines a specific issue in Hanafi family law: when a master marries off his slave girl, does he have to provide her with a separate house for her and her husband? The text from al-Hidayah provides a clear ruling with concise legal reasoning. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the interaction between the master's ownership rights and the husband's marital duties. Let's break down the passage step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is marrying off one's slave girl. The default ruling is that the master is not obliged to provide housing for her with her husband.
The Arabic phrase 'ليس عليه ان يبوئها بيت الزوج' explicitly negates obligation.
2
Understand the legal reasoning ('illa)
The master's right to use her services (istikhdam) remains after marriage. Providing a house would nullify that right.
The text says 'لان حق المولى فى الاستخدام باق' (because the master's right to service remains).
3
Note the exception
If the master voluntarily provides a house (tabwiyah), then the slave girl becomes entitled to nafaqah and housing from the husband.
The phrase 'فان بوأها معه بيتا فلها النفقة والسكنى' introduces the conditional exception.
4
Analyze the principle of ihtibas
Nafaqah is in exchange for restraint (ihtibas). If the master retains her for service, he owes nafaqah; if the husband has access, he owes it.
The text states 'لان النفقة تقابل الاحتباس' (nafaqah is in exchange for restraint).
5
Recognize the lasting nature of the master's right
Even after providing a house, the master can revert and take her services because ownership rights persist.
The text concludes 'فلا يسقط بالتبوية كما لا يسقط بالنكاح' (it is not nullified by tabwiyah just as it is not nullified by marriage).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جس نے اپنی باندی کا نکاح کیا تو رات خاوند کے گھر گذروانا آقا پر لازم نہیں ہے
ومن زوج امته فليس عليه ان يبوئها بيت الزوج ولكنها تخدم المولى ويقال للزوج متى ظفرت بها وطئتها لان حق المولى فى الاستخدام باق والتبوية ابطال له فان بوأها معه بيتا فلها النفقة والسكنى والافلا لان النفقة تقابل الاحتباس ولوبوأها بيتا تم بداله ان يستخدمها له ذلك لان الحق باق لبقاء الملک فلا يسقط بالتبوية كمالا يسقط بالنكاح
Whoever married off his slave girl, it is not obligatory upon him to provide her with a house with the husband. Rather, the slave girl serves the master, and it is said to the husband: whenever you get access to her, have intercourse with her. Because the master's right to service remains, and providing a house (tabwiyah) would nullify it. If he provides a house with him, then she has the right to maintenance and housing; otherwise not, because maintenance is in exchange for restraint. If he provides a house, then afterward it is permissible for him to employ her in service, because the right remains due to ownership and is not nullified by providing a house, just as it is not nullified by marriage.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on the obligations of a master who marries off his slave girl. The passage type is a ruling statement accompanied by legal reasoning (ta'lil). The ruling is that the master is not obligated to provide separate accommodation for the slave girl with her husband. The master retains the right to her service, and the husband is told to cohabit with her whenever possible. The reason ('illa) given is that the master's right to service is already established from the slave's status and continues after marriage; requiring the master to provide a house would effectively nullify that right. However, if the master voluntarily provides a house, then the slave girl becomes entitled to maintenance (nafaqah) and housing from the husband, because maintenance is in exchange for the wife's restraint (ihtibas) — if the master restrains her for his service, he owes maintenance; if the husband does, he owes it. Even if the master provides a house, he may later revoke that and take back her services, because his right remains due to ownership and is not lost by that gesture, just as it is not lost by marriage itself.

Key Terms
تبوية (tabwiyah): Providing a house or separate accommodation for the slave girl with her husband.
احتباس (ihtibas): Restraining the wife, i.e., having her available for the husband's exclusive sexual access.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The master's duty to house a married slave girl is not obligatory; it is a matter of choice, and the husband's obligation of nafaqah depends on whether the master provides housing.

Master's Right to Compel Slaves in Marriage: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Master's right to compel slaves to marry: Hanafi vs Shafi'i dispute with qiyas reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on compelling slaves to marry, explain the qiyas-based proof, and identify the exception for mukatab slaves.

Core Idea
A master can compel a full slave to marry to protect property; a mukatab cannot be compelled.

The Hanafi school holds that a master may compel both his male and female slaves to marry without their consent, viewing it as a reformation of the master's property by protecting the slave from zina. The Shafi'i school, and a weak narration from Abu Hanifah, argue that marriage is a human right and the male slave cannot be compelled. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the qiyas on female slaves, and the exception for mukatab slaves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Disputed Issue
The passage addresses whether a master can compel his adult male slave to marry without the slave's consent. The Hanafi position is that he can; the Shafi'i says he cannot.
The dispute extends to female slaves as well, but the focus is on the male slave.
2
State the Hanafi Position and Its Proof
The Hanafi position: the master can compel both male and female slaves. Proof: marriage is a reformation (islah) of the master's property because it protects the slave from zina, which causes loss. This is supported by qiyas on the female slave, whom the master can compel because he owns the usufruct of her vulva.
The qiyas is based on the illa of property protection.
3
State the Opposing Position and Its Proof
Shafi'i position: the male slave cannot be compelled. Proof: marriage is a characteristic of humanity; the slave is a human, not just property. The master owns him as property, not the right to marry him off without consent.
A weak narration from Abu Hanifah aligns with this view.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response and Qiyas Structure
The Hanafi response: the slave's sin harms the master's property, so the master's right of compulsion is derived from ownership. The qiyas: the male slave is like the female slave (asl) because both are property; the illa is protecting property from harm. However, the mukatab and mudabbar are exceptions because they are akin to free persons in disposition.
The mukatab's consent is required because he owns his earnings and is on the path to freedom.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf and Mufta Bihi
Practical difference: if a master marries his male slave without consent, in the Hanafi school the marriage is valid; in Shafi'i it is void. The mufta bihi is the Hanafi view; the opposing narration from Abu Hanifah is considered shadh (irregular).
The tarjih is based on stronger evidence and wider acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رضى الله عنه ذكر تزويج المولى عبده وامته ولم يذكررضا هما و هذا يرجع الى مذهبنا ان للولى اجبار هما على النكاح وعند الشافعى لا اجبار فى العبدو هو رواية عن ابي حنيفة لان النكاح من خصائص الأدمية والعبد داخل تحت ملك المولى من حيث انه مال فلایملک انکاحه بخلاف الامة لانه مالک منافع بضعها فیملک تمليكها ولنا ان الانكاح اصلاح ملكه لان فيه تحصينه عن الزناء الذي هو سبب الهلاك والنقصان فيملكه اعتبارا بالامة بخلاف المكاتب والمكاتبة لانهما التحقا بالاحرار تصرفا فيشترط رضا هما
He (the author) said: He (Imam Muhammad) mentioned the master marrying off his male slave and his female slave, but did not mention their consent. This goes back to our madhhab that the master has the right to compel both to marry. According to al-Shafi'i, there is no compulsion in the case of the male slave, and this is a narration from Abu Hanifah, because marriage is a characteristic of humanity, and the male slave is under the master's ownership as property, so he does not own his marriage — unlike the female slave, because he owns the usufruct of her vulva, so he can transfer that ownership. Our proof is that marriage is a reformation of his property, because it protects him from zina, which is a cause of destruction and loss, so he owns it by analogy to the female slave. Unlike the mudabbar or mukatab, because they have joined the free in disposition, so their consent is required.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the master's ability to compel his slaves into marriage without their consent. The Hanafi position is that the master can compel both his male and female slaves to marry, even without their explicit agreement, because marriage serves to reform the master's property by protecting the slave from zina, which harms the master's investment. The Shafi'i position, also reported as a weak narration from Abu Hanifah, differentiates: the male slave cannot be compelled because as a human he has autonomy in marriage, and he is the master's property only in a financial sense, so the master cannot marry him off against his will. However, for the female slave, the master owns the usufruct of her vulva, so he can transfer that right to a husband. As for a mukatab (slave with a contract of manumission) or mudabbar (slave promised freedom upon master's death), they are like free persons in disposition, so compulsion is not allowed. The tarjih (preferred view) is the Hanafi position that compulsion is valid for both full slaves.

Key Terms
إجبار (ijbar): The right of the master (or guardian) to compel the ward into marriage without explicit consent.
مكریب (mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract of kitabah with the master to earn and pay for his freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following slaves cannot be compelled to marry according to the Hanafi school?
A) Male full slave
B) Female full slave
C) Mukatab slave
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a master to compel full slaves to marry based on protecting property from harm, with the key exception of mukatab and mudabbar slaves who are treated as free in disposition.

Nikah — Marriage
advanced
12 min
Master's Right to Compel Marriage of Slaves and Tabwiyah
Explains master's right to compel marriage of slaves and effect of tabwiyah on nafaqah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a master's obligation to perform tabwiyah, and explain why the Hanafi school differs from al-Shafi'i regarding compelling a male slave to marry.

Core Idea
The master may compel both male and female slaves to marry without consent; tabwiyah is optional but triggers the husband's nafaqah.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah addresses two interrelated issues: the master's right to keep his slave-girl in service after marrying her off, and the master's authority to compel male and female slaves into marriage. While the first issue is straightforward—the master is never forced to hand over the slave-girl—the second involves a significant disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i. Understanding the Hanafi reasoning, based on property protection and qiyas, is essential for grasping the school's unique approach to slave marriage.

Guided Path
1
Define tabwiyah and the master's obligation
Understand what tabwiyah means and the ruling: the master is not required to surrender the slave-girl to her husband. The slave-girl continues to serve the master, and the husband may visit her.
The master's right to service remains despite the marriage.
2
Identify the effect of tabwiyah on nafaqah
If the master voluntarily settles the slave-girl with the husband, the husband becomes liable for her nafaqah and suknah. If the master later revokes, the obligation falls away.
Nafaqah is tied to confinement (ihitibas).
3
State the Hanafi position on compulsion (jabr)
The Hanafi madhhab holds that the master can marry off both male and female slaves without their consent. This is established in zahir al-riwayah, though a shadhdh narration from Abu Hanifa disagrees on the male slave.
The mukatab is exempt; his consent is required.
4
Analyze al-Shafi'i's objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the male slave cannot be compelled because marriage is a personal right; the master does not own the slave's person, only his service. Also, the slave can instantly divorce, making compulsion futile.
Al-Shafi'i allows compulsion for female slaves because the master owns the usufruct of her private part.
5
Follow the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafis reply that marriage protects the master's property: zina leads to hadd punishment, which can kill or wound the slave, destroying or damaging the master's asset. Hence, by qiyas on the slave-girl, the master may compel the male slave too.
The common cause (illah) is full ownership (milk al-raqaba) and the need to prevent loss.

مولی پر نہیں ۔ اور اگر مولیٰ نے باندی کو کسی مکان میں شوہر کے ساتھ شب باشی کا موقع دے دیا۔ پھر مولی کی رائے یہ ہوئی کہ اپنی باندی سے خدمت لیا کرے۔ تو اس کو یہ اختیار حاصل ہے کہ شب باشی کی اجازت منسوخ کر دے۔ اور شوہر سے نفقہ بھی ساقط ہو جائیگا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ مولیٰ کا حق خدمت بقاء ملک کی وجہ سے باقی ہے۔ لہذا جس طرح نکاح کر دینے سے یہ حق ساقط نہیں ہوا اسی طرح تبویہ سے بھی ساقط نہیں ہوگا۔ قال رضی اللہ عنہ سے صاحب ہدایہ نے کہا کہ امام محمد نے جامع صغیر میں یہ تو نہ ذکر کیا کہ مولیٰ اپنے غلام اور باندی کا نکاح کرسکتہ ہے۔ لیکن ان دونوں کی رضا مندی کا ذکر نہیں کیا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ ہمارا مذ حق میں جبر کرنے کا اختیار نہیں ہے۔ یہی ایک روایت امام ابوحنیفہ سے ہے جس کو طحاوی نے روایت کیا ہے۔ اگر چہ یہ روایت شاذ ہے۔ امام شافعی کے مذہب کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ اگر مولیٰ نے بغیر غلام کی رضامندی کے نکاح کر دیا وہ نافذ نہیں ہوگا البتہ باندی کا نکاح بغیر اس کی رضا مندی کے بالا جماع درست اور نافذ ہے۔ امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ نکاح آدمی ہونے کا خاصہ ہے اور غلام مولی کی ملک کے تحت داخل ہے۔ مال ہونے کی نکاح اسی طرح مشکی ہو گا جس طرح اجنبی کا ۔ بخلاف باندی کے۔ کیونکہ مولی باندی کے منافع بضع کا مالک ہے۔ لہذا بغیر اس کی رضا مندی کے دوسرے کو بھی مالک بنا سکتا ہے۔ اسلئے کہ یہ خالص اپنی ملک میں تصرف کرنا ہے۔ امام شافعی کی دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ غلام کا نکاح بغیر اس کی رضا مندی کے غیر مفید ہے کیونکہ غلام کو فی الحال طلاق دینے کا اختیار ہے۔ لہذا بغیر اس کی رضامندی کے نکاح کرنے سے مقصود نکاح حاصل نہیں ہو گا۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ نکاح کرنا اپنی ملک کی اصلاح کرنا ہے کیونکہ نکاح کرنے میں غلام کو زنا سے محفوظ رکھنا ہے جو ہلاکت اور نقصان کا سبب ہے۔ یعنی جب حد لگائی جائے گی تو وہ حد کبھی ہلاک کر دے گی اور کبھی زخم پیدا کر دے گی ۔ پنس پہلی صورت میں مال کو ہلاک کرنا ہے اور دوسری صورت میں مال کو ناقص کرنا ہے۔ لہذا مولی بغیر غلام کی رضا مندی کے نکاح کرنے کا مالک ہے باندی پر قیاس کرتے ہوئے اور جامع دونوں کے درمیان سبب ولایت کا موجود ہوتا ہے یعنی ملک رقبہ کا حاصل ہونا اور اپنی ملک کو زنا سے محفوظ رکھنا جو موجب ہلاکت اور نقصان ہے۔ برخلاف مکاتب اور مکاتبہ کے کہ بغیر ان کی رضا مندی کے مولی ان کا نکاح نہیں کر سکتا ہے دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ دونوں آزاد کے ساتھ لاحق ہو گئے ہیں۔ ملک ید اور ملک تصرف کے اعتبار سے۔ پس اگر مولی کو بغیر ان کی رضامندی کے نکاح کا اختیار دے دیا جائے تو ان دونوں کے ملک تصرف کو فوت کردینا لازم آئے گا جو درست نہیں ہے۔ اسلئے مکاتب اور مکاتبہ کی رضا مندی کو شرط قرار دیا گیا ہے۔
The meaning of tabwiyah is that the slave-girl is handed over to the husband and the master stops taking service from her. If the slave-girl goes back and forth to the husband and also serves the master, this will not be tabwiyah. The case is: when a master marries off his slave-girl to someone, it is not obligatory upon the master to settle her in the husband's house or to abandon his right to her service. Rather, the slave-girl will continue to serve the master, and the husband will be told to have intercourse with her when his opportunity comes. The proof is that the master's right to service remains; giving permission for marriage does not terminate the master's right, and settling the slave-girl in the husband's house would nullify the master's right. Thus, in the case of tabwiyah, nullifying the higher right would be necessary due to the lower right, and that is not permissible. Therefore, tabwiyah is not obligatory upon the master. However, if the master does settle the slave-girl with the husband in a house—i.e., gives permission for her to stay at night in that house—then the husband becomes obligated to provide maintenance and housing. If the master does not give permission for her to stay with the husband, then the husband is not obligated for maintenance or housing. The proof is that maintenance is the counterpart of confinement. So if the master retains her for his own service, maintenance is on the master, not the husband; if he releases her for the husband's service, maintenance is on the husband, not the master. If the master allows the slave-girl to stay with the husband at night in a house, and then later decides to take service from her, he has the right to revoke that permission, and the husband's obligation for maintenance falls away. The proof is that the master's right to service remains due to the continuation of ownership. Just as this right was not terminated by marriage, so it is not terminated by tabwiyah. The author of Hidayah said that Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir mentioned that the master can marry off his slave and slave-girl, but did not mention their consent. The author of Hidayah states: This is our madhhab—that the master can compel his slave and slave-girl to marry. The meaning of compulsion is that if the master marries them off without their consent, the marriage takes effect. According to Imam al-Shafi'i, he does not have the right to compel the male slave. There is a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa transmitted by al-Tahawi, though it is shadhdh. The summary of al-Shafi'i's madhhab is that if the master marries a male slave without his consent, it is not effective; however, a slave-girl's marriage without her consent is valid and effective. Al-Shafi'i's proof is that marriage is a characteristic of being human, and the slave is under the master's ownership as property; therefore, the master does not own his marriage. Thus, the master's action of marrying him is a disposition in something he does not own, so it is defective like a stranger's action, unlike the slave-girl because the master owns the usufruct of her private part, so he can transfer ownership without her consent—this is pure disposition in his own property. Al-Shafi'i's second proof is that the male slave's marriage without his consent is futile because he has the immediate right of divorce, so the purpose of marriage is not achieved without his consent. Our proof is that marriage is a reform of one's property because it protects the slave from zina, which causes destruction and damage—since the hadd punishment may kill or wound, thus destroying or damaging the property. Therefore, the master has the right to marry the male slave without his consent, by analogy to the slave-girl, and the common cause of wilaya exists: full ownership (milk al-raqaba) and protecting one's property from zina which leads to destruction. Contrary to the mukatab slave and slave-girl, the master cannot marry them without their consent because they have become akin to free persons in terms of milk al-yad and milk al-tasarruf; giving the master that authority would remove their right of disposition, which is not valid.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah deals with two related issues: the master's obligation regarding tabwiyah (settling the slave-girl with her husband) and the master's right to compel slaves into marriage. The passage begins by defining tabwiyah: it is not merely allowing the slave-girl to visit; it requires the master to relinquish his right to her service and hand her over entirely to the husband. The author rules that a master is not obligated to perform tabwiyah—he may keep the slave-girl in his own service and tell the husband to visit her whenever possible. The rationale is that the master's right to service is preexisting and marriage permission does not nullify it; forcing tabwiyah would subordinate the master's higher right to the husband's lower right. However, if the master voluntarily chooses to allow the slave-girl to live with the husband, then the husband becomes liable for her nafaqah (maintenance) and suknah (housing), since nafaqah compensates for confinement. If the master later changes his mind, he can revoke the permission and the husband's obligation ceases. The passage then addresses the master's authority to compel marriage. The Hanafi position (except for a shadhdh narration from Abu Hanifa reported by al-Tahawi) is that the master can marry off both male and female slaves without their consent, because marriage is a reform of property (protecting the slave from zina and its harmful consequences). This applies equally to male and female slaves, by analogy from the female slave. Imam al-Shafi'i distinguishes between them: he holds that the master cannot compel the male slave because marriage is a personal right of the human being, and the slave is not fully owned in that respect; but he can compel the female slave because her sexual usufruct is owned. The Hanafi rebuttal uses qiyas: just as the master can marry off the slave-girl without her consent to protect his property from loss, similarly he can marry off the male slave to avoid the risk of hadd punishment damaging his property. The exception is the mukatab (a slave who has contracted manumission), because he has acquired a measure of personal capacity akin to freedom, so his consent is required.

Key Terms
تبویہ (tabwiyah): The act of the master settling a married slave-girl in her husband's house, releasing her from service and allowing her to live with him.
جبر (jabr): The authority to compel a slave into marriage without their consent.
مکاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract of manumission with the master, paying in installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, is the master obligated to perform tabwiyah (settling a married slave-girl with her husband)?
A) Yes, it is wajib upon the master.
B) No, it is not obligatory; the master may keep her in his service.
C) It depends on the husband's request.
D) It is obligatory only if the husband is poor.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows a master to compel both male and female slaves (except mukatab) into marriage without consent, based on the principle that marriage protects the master's property from loss due to zina; tabwiyah is optional and its performance transfers nafaqah liability to the husband.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate

Master Kills His Slave-Girl Before Consummation: Mahr Due or Not?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether mahr is due when master kills his slave-girl before consummation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two intra-Hanafi positions on mahr when a master kills his slave-girl before consummation, explain the analogical reasoning (qiyas) used by each side, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
When a master kills his slave-girl before consummation, Abu Hanifa says no mahr; the Sahibayn say mahr is due.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding mahr liability when a master kills his own slave-girl after marrying her off but before consummation. The dispute hinges on whether the master's killing should be equated with natural death (thus mahr due) or treated as a deliberate prevention of delivery (thus forfeiting mahr). We will explore the proofs for each position and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
State the case and the two positions
Identify the scenario: a master marries off his slave-girl, then kills her before consummation. Note the two positions: Abu Hanifa says no mahr; Sahibayn say mahr is due.
The positions are opposite, both within the Hanafi school.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa draws an analogy (qiyas) with a free woman who apostatizes before consummation: the wife's apostasy prevents delivery of the marital benefit (mubdal), so she forfeits mahr. Similarly, the master prevented delivery, so he loses the mahr.
Key concept: prevention of delivery (man' al-mubdal) before taslim.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn analogize to two cases: (1) natural death of the slave-girl before consummation triggers mahr by consensus; (2) killing by a stranger also leaves mahr intact. The master's killing is considered death at the appointed term (ajal), so the same rule applies.
They rely on the principle that the killed person dies at his appointed term (میت باجله).
4
Compare the illas and the thamarah al-khilaf
Abu Hanifa focuses on the act of prevention by the master; the Sahibayn focus on the fact of death. The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, husband pays nothing; under Sahibayn, husband pays full mahr to the master.
This is a clear example of ikhtilaf in ta'lil (legal reasoning).
5
Identify the mufta bihi
Later scholars (e.g., in the Hidayah) typically prefer the Sahibayn's view. The mufta bihi is that mahr is due from the husband to the master.
Tarjih authority: the text states the Sahibayn's position as the relied-upon one.
